Wisdom Behind Half of One-Tenth Diyah Per Tooth
Question and answer on the specific amount of diyah per tooth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the talil reasoning behind the Hanafi fixed diyah of half of one-tenth per tooth and apply the principle of averaging and dual compensation to similar cases.

Core Idea
Diyah per tooth is half of one-tenth because of average tooth count (30) and compensation for both function and beauty.

In Hanafi fiqh, diyah for bodily organs follows a proportional scale: single organs get full diyah, paired organs get half, etc. But for teeth, which are numerous and vary in count, the fixed amount of half of one-tenth (500 dirhams) seems inconsistent with a simple count-based proportion. This passage explores the wisdom (hikmah) behind this fixed amount, revealing that it factors in the average number of teeth (30) and compensates both the loss of chewing function and facial beauty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent inconsistency
Note that if teeth were treated like fingers (1/10 each for 10 fingers), 32 teeth would give a much smaller per-tooth amount. The text calculates an expected 1/4 of 1/8 (= 312.5 dirhams) vs. the actual 500 dirhams.
The expected amount from a simple count-based analogy is 1/32 of full diyah, which is 312.5 dirhams.
2
Recognize the variation in tooth count
People may have 28, 30, or 32 teeth. The Hanafis take the average (30) as the basis for the calculation.
The average of 30 is the midpoint between 28 and 32.
3
Understand the dual basis of diyah for teeth
Diyah compensates for loss of function (chewing) and beauty (appearance). Each tooth contributes to both, so the compensation must account for both losses.
Function and beauty are assessed independently.
4
Follow the composite calculation
For a single tooth: functional loss is 1/30 of full diyah (since 30 teeth each contribute equally to function). Beauty loss is half of that (1/60). Combined: 1/30 + 1/60 = 1/20 = half of one-tenth.
The full diyah is 10,000 dirhams; half of one-tenth is 500 dirhams.
5
Apply the fixed amount uniformly
The fixed amount applies regardless of whether the tooth is an incisor or molar, as the averaging and dual compensation cover all types.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1]قوله : فينبغي... الخ ؛ يعني إذا كان إتلافُ الأعضاء التي خُلِقَت في كلّ شخص آحاداً كالأنف موجباً للدية الكاملة كدِيَة النفس، والتي خُلقت مثاني كاليدين والرجلين يوجب في كلّ واحدٍ منهما نصفُ الدية، والتي خُلِقَت أرباعاً كالأشفار يوجب في كل واحدة منها ربعها، والتي خُلِقَت عشرة كالأصابع يوجب في كل واحدة منها عشرها ، وهو ألف درهم شرعي. ففي الأسنان التي خُلِقَت اثنتين وثلاثين ينبغي أن يجب في كل واحدة منها ربع ثمن الدية الذي هو ثلاثمئة واثنا عشر درهماً ونصف دون نصف العشر الذي هو خمسمئة فما الحكمة في وجوب هذا دون ذاك ؟ حاصل الجواب : إن الأسنان لها ثلاثة مراتب، فإنّها قد تنبت لبعض الناس اثنان وثلاثون سناً، ولبعضهم ثلاثون ولبعضهم ثمان وعشرون، والوسط ثلاثون، فاعتبرنا الوسط، والدية تجب بتفويت المنفعة أو الجمال، فإذا سقط سن فاتت منفعته بالكلية، فينبغي أن يجب عشر ثلث الدية، وفات نصف منفعة السن الواحدة المقابل لها. فينبغي أن ينصف دية السن الواحدة، وكذلك الجمال، فإذا جمعناهما بلغ المجموع نصف العشر، فهذه حكمة وجوب نصف العشر، هذا كله مبني على وجوب نصف العشر في كل حال من الأحوال الثلاث. وأما على قول الأكمل له : إذا ضرب رجل رجلاً حتى سقطت أسنانه كلها
His saying: 'Thus it should be...' means: When the destruction of organs that are created singly in each person, like the nose, incurs full diyah like the diyah of the soul; and those created in pairs like hands and feet incurs for each one half diyah; and those created in fours like eyelids incurs for each one quarter; and those created in tens like fingers incurs for each one one-tenth, which is one thousand legal dirhams. Then for teeth, which are created as thirty-two, it should be that for each one a quarter of one-eighth of the diyah is due, which is three hundred and twelve and a half dirhams, rather than half of one-tenth which is five hundred. So what is the wisdom in making half of one-tenth obligatory instead of that? The summary of the answer: Teeth have three levels: some people grow thirty-two teeth, some thirty, some twenty-eight, and the average is thirty, so we consider the average. Diyah is imposed for loss of function or beauty. So when a tooth falls, its function is completely lost, so one-tenth of one-third of the diyah should be due, and half of the function of a single tooth is lost correspondingly. So it should be half of the diyah of one tooth. Similarly for beauty. When we combine them, the total reaches half of one-tenth. This is the wisdom for the obligation of half of one-tenth. This is all based on the obligation of half of one-tenth in all three cases. As for the statement of al-Akmal: 'If a man strikes a man until all his teeth fall out...'
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage addresses a question about the fixed diyah for each tooth: half of one-tenth of the full diyah (i.e., 500 dirhams). The question arises because if we apply a proportional analogy based on the number of organs, we would expect for 32 teeth a quarter of one-eighth (312.5 dirhams). The commentator explains the wisdom: the number of teeth varies between 28, 30, and 32; the average is 30. Diyah for teeth compensates for both function (chewing) and beauty. For the average of 30 teeth, the functional loss of one tooth is assessed as a proportion of one-third of the diyah (since there are roughly three functional categories: front teeth, etc.), and the beauty loss is half of that. Combining these two gives half of one-tenth. The answer shows that the amount is not arbitrary but based on a composite of function and beauty, averaged over the population. The passage ends with a reference to al-Akmal's statement about a case where all teeth fall out, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
نصف العشر (nisf al-'ushr): Half of one-tenth of the full diyah, i.e., 500 dirhams.
المنفعة والجمال (al-manfa'ah wa al-jamal): Function and beauty; diyah for teeth compensates for both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because all teeth are equally valuable
B) Because it averages out to account for variation in number of teeth and loss of function and beauty
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) set that amount
D) Because the majority of people have 32 teeth

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fixed diyah per tooth of half of one-tenth (500 dirhams) is based on averaging the varying number of teeth (30) and compensating for both loss of function and beauty.

Wisdom Behind the Diya for Teeth: Half a Tenth
Explaining the rationale for teeth diya being half a tenth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school sets the diya for a tooth at half a tenth and apply the reasoning to similar cases.

Core Idea
Teeth diya is half a tenth because the median tooth count is 30 and each tooth loses its own benefit plus half of its opposite's chewing benefit.

At first glance, if teeth number 32, one might expect each tooth's diya to be 1/32 of the full amount. But the established ruling is half a tenth (1/20). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi jurists' wisdom behind this apparent discrepancy, combining transmitted evidence (hadith) with rational analysis of benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Note the general pattern: limbs created singly (nose) = full diya, in pairs = half, in quadruples = quarter, in tens = tenth. Since teeth are 32, one would expect 1/32 per tooth. But the actual ruling is 1/20. Define the problem clearly.
The expected fraction is 1/32 of the full diya (e.g., 312.5 dirhams). The actual is 1/20 (500 dirhams).
2
Consider the median tooth count
Recognize that not everyone has 32 teeth; the number varies from 28 to 32. The Hanafi jurists use the median number of 30 as the basis for calculation.
This is a type of istihsan (juristic preference) considering human variation.
3
Analyze the dual benefits of teeth
Teeth provide two distinct benefits: mastication (chewing) and adornment (appearance). When a tooth is lost, its own benefit is entirely gone, and half of the opposite tooth's chewing benefit is also lost because chewing requires opposing teeth.
The other half of the opposite tooth (adornment) remains.
4
Calculate the combined loss
With 30 teeth, the benefit per tooth is 1/30 of total benefit. Half of that benefit (from the opposite tooth) is 1/60. Sum them: 1/30 + 1/60 = 1/20. This matches the half-a-tenth ruling.
This calculation is a rational justification (hikmah) supporting the transmitted ruling.
5
Confirm with the hadith
The hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm states 'In a tooth, five camels,' which is exactly half a tenth of the full diya (100 camels). The wisdom aligns with this explicit textual evidence.
The hadith is the primary dalil; the hikmah demonstrates its congruence with rational principles.

كتاب الديات/ أنواعها وأحكامها وفي كلِّ أصبع يد أو رجل عشرها، وفي كل مفصل من إصبع فيها مفاصل ثلث عشرها، وفيما فيه مفصلان نصفُ عشرها، كما في كل سن) فإنَّ فيها نصف العشر لما كان عدد الأسنان اثنين وثلاثين، فينبغي أن يجب في كلِّ سن ربع ثمن الدية، فما الحكمة في وجوب نصف العشر. واحد. كذا في «الأكملية»، وفي واحد منها ربع الدية ; لأنّ الأشفار أربعة، وفي اثنين نصف الدية، وفي الثلاثة ثلاثة أرباع كذا في «التبيين» [١]قوله : فينبغي... الخ ; يعني إذا كان إتلافُ الأعضاء التي خُلِقَت في كلّ شخص آحاداً كالأنف موجباً للدية الكاملة كدِيَة النفس، والتي خُلقت مثاني كاليدين والرجلين يوجب في كلّ واحدٍ منهما نصفُ الدية، والتي خُلِقَت أرباعاً كالأشفار يوجب في كل واحدة منها ربعها، والتي خُلِقَت عشرة كالأصابع يوجب في كل واحدة منها عشرها ، وهو ألف درهم شرعي. ففي الأسنان التي خُلِقَت اثنتين وثلاثين ينبغي أن يجب في كل واحدة منها ربع ثمن الدية الذي هو ثلاثمئة واثنا عشر درهماً ونصف دون نصف العشر الذي هو خمسمئة فما الحكمة في وجوب هذا دون ذاك ? حاصل الجواب : إن الأسنان لها ثلاثة مراتب، فإنّها قد تنبت لبعض الناس اثنان وثلاثون سناً، ولبعضهم ثلاثون ولبعضهم ثمان وعشرون، والوسط ثلاثون، فاعتبرنا الوسط، والدية تجب بتفويت المنفعة أو الجمال، فإذا سقط سن فاتت منفعته بالكلية، فينبغي أن يجب عشر ثلث الدية، وفات نصف منفعة السن الواحدة المقابل لها. فينبغي أن ينصف دية السن الواحدة، وكذلك الجمال، فإذا جمعناهما بلغ المجموع نصف العشر، فهذه حكمة وجوب نصف العشر، هذا كله مبني على وجوب نصف العشر في كل حال من الأحوال الثلاث. وأما على قول الأكمل له : إذا ضرب رجل رجلاً حتى سقطت أسنانه كلها وكل عضو ذَهَبَ نفعُهُ بضرب ففيه ديته كيد شلت، وعين عميت. فيخطر ببالي : أنَّ عدد الأسنان وإن كان اثنين وثلاثين، فالأربعة الأخيرة وهي أسنان الحلم قد لا تنبت لبعض الناس، وقد تنبت لبعض الناس منها بعضها ، وللبعض كلها، فالعدد المتوسط للأسنان ثلاثون، ثم للأسنان منفعتان الزينة والمضغ، فإذا سقط سن يبطل منفعتها بالكلية، ونصف منفعة السن التي تقابلها ، وهو منفعة المضغ، وإن كانَ النّصف الآخر وهو الزينة باقية، وإذا كان العــدد المتوسط ثلاثين، فمنفعةُ السِّنُ الواحدة ثلث العشر، ونصف المنفعة سدس العشر، ومجموعهما نصف العشر، والله أعلم بالحقيقة. (وكل عضو ذَهَبَ نفعُهُ بضرب ففيه ديته كيد شلت، وعين عميت. كانت عليه دِيَة وثلاثة أخماس الدِّيَة، وهي من الدراهم ستة عشر ألف درهم، بناءً على كونها اثنين وثلاثين، كما هو المشهور، فلا مخالفة أصلاً كما لا يخفى. فإن قيل : فيزيد على دِيَّة كاملة، مع أنّ إتلافها إتلافُ النفس من وجه، فزيادة حكمه على الإتلاف من كل وجه غير معقول. قلنا : نعم ; لكن ثبت هذا بما روي عن عمرو بن حزم له وهو قوله : «وفي السن خمس من الإبل»(۱) مخالفاً للقياس فلا إشكال، قال في «العناية»(۲) : وليس في البدن جنس عضو يجب بتقويمه أكثر من مقدار الدية سوى الأسنان. [١] قوله : ومجموعهما... الخ ; فعلى هذا ديةُ كلِّ سن من الإبل خمسة، ومن الدنانير خمسون، ومن الدراهم خمسمئة ، ففي الأسنان المعتبرة إذا أتلف كلها مئة وخمسون إبلاً ، أو ألف وخمسمئة دينار، أو خمسة عشر ألف درهم.
Book of Blood Money / Its Types and Rulings. And for each finger of hand or foot, one tenth; for each joint of a finger that has joints, one third of one tenth; and for a finger with two joints, half of one tenth, like in every tooth: for it has half of one tenth because the number of teeth is thirty-two. So it should be that in every tooth one quarter of one eighth of the blood money is obligatory. What is the wisdom in the obligation of half of one tenth? One. Thus in 'al-Akmaliyya', and in one of them one quarter of the blood money because the eyelids are four; in two, half; in three, three-quarters, thus in 'al-Tabyin'. [1] His saying: 'so it should...' etc.; meaning: if the destruction of limbs created singly in each person (like the nose) obligates the full blood money (diya) like the blood money of life, and those created in pairs (like hands and feet) obligate half the blood money in each, and those created in quadruples (like eyelids) obligate one quarter in each, and those created in tens (like fingers) obligate one tenth in each, which is one thousand legal dirhams. Then in teeth, which are created as thirty-two, it should be that in each one one quarter of one eighth of the blood money is obligatory, which is three hundred and twelve and a half dirhams, not half of one tenth which is five hundred. So what is the wisdom in obligating this rather than that? The summary of the answer: Teeth have three levels; sometimes they grow for some people as thirty-two teeth, for some thirty, and for some twenty-eight. The middle is thirty, so we considered the middle. The blood money is due for loss of benefit or beauty. So when a tooth falls, its benefit is entirely lost; it should be obligatory that one tenth of one third of the blood money, and half of the benefit of the opposite tooth is lost, so half of the blood money of one tooth should be due, and similarly for beauty. When we combine them, the total reaches half of one tenth. This is the wisdom of the obligation of half of one tenth. All this is based on the obligation of half of one tenth in every situation of the three states. As for the statement of al-Akmal: If a man strikes another until all his teeth fall out, and every limb whose benefit is lost due to a blow has its full blood money, like a paralyzed hand, blinded eye. It occurs to me: The number of teeth, even if thirty-two, the last four which are wisdom teeth may not grow for some people, and for some some of them grow, and for some all; so the average number of teeth is thirty. Then teeth have two benefits: adornment and chewing. So when a tooth falls, its benefit is entirely lost, and half of the benefit of the opposite tooth, which is chewing, is lost, while the other half (adornment) remains. Since the average number is thirty, the benefit of one tooth is one third of one tenth, and half of that is one sixth, and their total is half of one tenth. And Allah knows best the reality. (And every limb whose benefit is lost by a blow has its full blood money, like a paralyzed hand, blinded eye. On him is the blood money plus three-fifths of it, which is sixteen thousand dirhams, based on its being thirty-two, as is well-known, so there is no contradiction at all as is not hidden. If it is said: This exceeds a full blood money, while its destruction is in one respect destruction of life, so its ruling exceeding destruction in every respect is unreasonable. We say: Yes; but this is established by what is narrated from 'Amr ibn Hazm, his saying: 'And in a tooth, five camels,' contrary to analogy, so there is no problem. Al-'Inayah said: There is no type of limb in the body whose assessment exceeds the amount of the blood money except teeth. [1] His saying: 'and their total...' etc.; so on this, the blood money of each tooth is five camels, or fifty dinars, or five hundred dirhams. So in the considered teeth, if all are destroyed, (it is) one hundred and fifty camels, or one thousand five hundred dinars, or fifteen thousand dirhams.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling for the blood money (diya) of teeth, specifically addressing why each tooth is valued at half a tenth (nisf al-'ushr) of the full diya, rather than the mathematically expected one-quarter of one-eighth (rub' thumn). The commentator first notes the pattern: limbs created singly (e.g., nose) carry full diya, in pairs (e.g., hands) half, in quadruples (e.g., eyelids) quarter, in tens (e.g., fingers) tenth. Since teeth are thirty-two in some people, one would expect each tooth to be one-fourth of one-eighth (i.e., 1/32 of full diya), but the actual ruling is half a tenth (1/20). The commentator seeks the wisdom (hikmah) behind this. The answer given is based on the median number of teeth (30) and the dual benefits of teeth: mastication and appearance. When a tooth is lost, its own benefit is entirely gone, but half the benefit of the opposing tooth (chewing) is lost as well; the other half (appearance) remains. Since the median number is 30, the benefit per tooth is 1/30 of the total benefit. Half of that is 1/60, and adding the tooth's own 1/30 gives 1/20, i.e., half a tenth. This rational justification is consistent with the ruling established by the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm (may Allah be pleased with him) that in a tooth is five camels, which amounts to half a tenth of the full diya. The passage also includes a discussion that the total diya for all teeth exceeds a full diya, but this is accepted as a special case, contrary to qiyas, due to the explicit sunna.

Key Terms
دية (diya): Financial compensation paid to the victim or heirs for bodily harm or homicide; in this context, the full amount for a life is 100 camels (or equivalent: 10,000 dirhams or 1,000 dinars).
نصف العشر (nisf al-'ushr): Half of one tenth; i.e., 1/20 of the full diya. For teeth, it is 5 camels, 50 dinars, or 500 dirhams.
سن (sinn): Tooth; in fiqh, each tooth is considered independently for diya.
منفعة (manfa'ah): Benefit or utility; for teeth, the two benefits are mastication (chewing) and adornment (appearance). Diya compensates for loss of benefit or beauty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the wisdom (hikmah) behind the diya for a tooth being half a tenth rather than one thirty-second of the full diya?
A) Because the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm says so, and there is no rational explanation.
B) Because teeth have three benefits: appearance, chewing, and speech.
C) Because the median number of teeth is 30, and each tooth has its own benefit plus half the benefit of the opposite tooth, totaling 1/20 of the full diya.
D) Because teeth are created in pairs and the diya for pairs is half, so half of one tenth is correct.

Key Takeaway
The diya for a tooth is half a tenth (1/20 of full diya) based on the median count of 30 teeth and the dual loss of benefit and beauty, confirmed by the explicit hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm.

Diyat — Blood Money

Diyah of a Tooth: Ruling and Proof from Hadith
Explaining the diyah of each tooth and total calculation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah of a tooth according to the Hanafi school and explain its proof from the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm.

Core Idea
Each tooth has a diyah of 5 camels, even though the total exceeds a full life diyah — established by hadith overriding analogy.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the diyah (blood money) for teeth, a unique case where compensation for a single body part can exceed the full diyah of a person. The author clarifies the number of teeth considered and provides the proof from the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm, which sets the diyah at five camels per tooth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The passage begins with a general rule: any organ whose function is lost due to a blow receives its full diyah. Understand that this is the baseline for bodily injuries.
2
Understand the specific case of teeth
The author discusses the number of teeth. Though the well-known count is 32, the last four wisdom teeth may not appear in everyone, so an average of 30 is used for calculation. Note the two benefits of teeth: adornment and chewing.
This average affects the fraction when calculating partial loss.
3
Learn the diyah amount per tooth
The hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm states: 'In a tooth, five camels.' This is the textual proof. From this, the diyah per tooth is fixed at 5 camels, or equivalent in dinars (50) or dirhams (500).
This ruling overrides qiyas (analogy) because the hadith is specific.
4
Calculate the total diyah for all teeth
Using 30 teeth, the total diyah is 30 × 5 = 150 camels (or 1500 dinars, 15000 dirhams). Note that this exceeds a full diyah (100 camels) for a person, which is exceptional.
The author cites al-'Inayah that teeth are the only body part where the valuation exceeds the full diyah.
5
Recognize the exceptional nature and justification
The objection that destroying all teeth should not exceed a full life diyah is answered: the hadith establishes it contrary to analogy, so no problem remains.
This demonstrates that a clear textual proof can override general principles.

(وكل عضو ذَهَبَ نفعُهُ بضرب ففيه ديته كيد شلت، وعين عميت. كانت عليه دِيَة وثلاثة أخماس الدِّيَة، وهي من الدراهم ستة عشر ألف درهم، بناءً على كونها اثنين وثلاثين، كما هو المشهور، فلا مخالفة أصلاً كما لا يخفى. فإن قيل : فيزيد على دِيَّة كاملة، مع أنّ إتلافها إتلافُ النفس من وجه، فزيادة حكمه على الإتلاف من كل وجه غير معقول.
قلنا : نعم ؛ لكن ثبت هذا بما روي عن عمرو بن حزم له وهو قوله : «وفي السن خمس من الإبل»(۱) مخالفاً للقياس فلا إشكال، قال في «العناية»(۲) : وليس في البدن جنس عضو يجب بتقويمه أكثر من مقدار الدية سوى الأسنان. [١] قوله : ومجموعهما... الخ ؛ فعلى هذا ديةُ كلِّ سن من الإبل خمسة، ومن الدنانير خمسون، ومن الدراهم خمسمئة ، ففي الأسنان المعتبرة إذا أتلف كلها مئة وخمسون إبلاً ، أو ألف وخمسمئة دينار، أو خمسة عشر ألف درهم.
Every organ whose benefit is lost by a blow has its full diyah, like a paralyzed hand and a blinded eye. It occurs to me: although the number of teeth is thirty-two, the last four, which are the wisdom teeth, may not grow for some people, and for some people some of them may grow, and for others all of them. So the average number of teeth is thirty. Then teeth have two benefits: adornment and chewing. When a tooth falls out, its benefit is completely lost, and half the benefit of the tooth opposite it, which is the benefit of chewing, is lost, while the other half, which is adornment, remains. Since the average number is thirty, the benefit of one tooth is one-third of a tenth, and half the benefit is one-sixth of a tenth, and their total is one-half of a tenth. And Allah knows the truth. (Every organ whose benefit is lost by a blow has its full diyah, like a paralyzed hand and a blinded eye. Upon it is diyah and three-fifths of diyah, which in dirhams is sixteen thousand dirhams, based on it being thirty-two, as is well-known, so there is no contradiction at all, as is not hidden. For if it is said: This exceeds a full diyah, while destroying it is destroying a life in one aspect, so increasing its ruling over complete destruction is unreasonable. We say: Yes, but this is established by what was narrated from 'Amr ibn Hazm, which is his statement: 'In a tooth, five camels,' contrary to analogy, so there is no problem. He said in al-'Inayah: There is no bodily organ whose valuation exceeds the amount of diyah except teeth. [1] His statement: Their total... etc.; so on this, the diyah of each tooth from camels is five, from dinars fifty, and from dirhams five hundred. So in the considered teeth, if all are destroyed, it is one hundred fifty camels, or one thousand five hundred dinars, or fifteen thousand dirhams.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the diyah (blood money) for teeth, with its proof from the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm. It begins by stating a general principle: any organ whose function is lost due to a blow receives full diyah. Then the commentator focuses on teeth, first discussing the number of teeth. The well-known count is 32, but the last four wisdom teeth may not appear in everyone. So an average of 30 is used. Teeth have two benefits: adornment and chewing. If a tooth is lost, its chewing benefit is lost and half the benefit of the opposite tooth (chewing) is also lost, while adornment remains. Based on the average of 30, each tooth's benefit is 1/30 of the total, half of which is 1/60. The commentator then quotes the matn: for a tooth, the diyah is 5 camels (from the hadith), which gives 50 dinars or 500 dirhams per tooth. For all 30 teeth, the total is 150 camels, 1500 dinars, or 15,000 dirhams. The hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm is cited as the proof, overriding the qiyas (analogy) that would suggest a lesser amount because destroying all teeth is like destroying a life in one respect. The text from al-'Inayah notes that teeth are the only body part where the total diyah exceeds a full diyah. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money compensation for bodily harm or death; here, for a tooth it is fixed at 5 camels.
سن (sinn): Tooth; the passage discusses the number of teeth and the calculation of diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 30
C) 28
D) 36

Key Takeaway
The diyah for a single tooth is 5 camels, fixed by the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm, and the total for all teeth (150 camels) exceeds a full diyah, illustrating that textual proof can override analogy.

Fixed Diyat Amounts for Specific Head Wounds
Ruling on fixed diyat for six head wounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fixed diyat amounts for the six specific head wounds (khidash, hashimah, munqilah, ammah, jaifah, penetrating jaifah) and explain the proofs from hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Six head wounds have fixed diyat fractions from hadith; a penetrating jaifah gets two-thirds via qiyas as two jaifahs.

This passage from Al-Hidayah enumerates the fixed diyat amounts for particular head wounds, distinguishing them by severity. These amounts are based on explicit hadith from the Book of Amr ibn Hazm and a narration from Zaid ibn Thabit, with the penetrating jaifah amount derived by qiyas. Understanding these fixed fractions is essential for accurate application of diyat law.

Guided Path
1
List the six wounds and their amounts
Identify each wound type: khidash (1/20 diyat), hashimah (1/10), munqilah (3/20), ammah (1/3), jaifah (1/3), penetrating jaifah (2/3). Note that ammah and jaifah have the same fraction.
Penetrating jaifah is a special case.
2
Classify the wounds by severity
Order them from least to most severe: khidash (skin scratch), hashimah (bone broken), munqilah (bone displaced), ammah (membrane reached), jaifah (belly cavity reached), penetrating jaifah (through and through).
This classification affects the diyat amount.
3
Examine the textual proofs
The matn is supported by the hadith from the Book of Amr ibn Hazm: 'In the ma'mumah (ammah) one third, in the jaifah one third, in the munqilah fifteen (camels).' Also, a narration from Zaid ibn Thabit gives similar values. Identify that these are ahad hadith with zanni thubut but qati dalala.
These proofs fix the amounts as Sunnah.
4
Analyze the qiyas for penetrating jaifah
The penetrating jaifah is analogized to two separate jaifahs: one from the front and one from the back. Each single jaifah is 1/3, so the penetrating gets 2/3. This is a clear qiyas with a jali illah (shared cause of being a cavity-penetrating wound).
This qiyas is accepted by the Hanafi school.
5
Note the scope of fixed amounts
These six wounds have fixed diyat fractions; other wounds (e.g., mudihah) have their own fixed amounts, but most wounds are subject to arbitration (hukumat). This passage covers only the named wounds.
Memorize these six fractions for exams.

( والامة والجائفةُ ثلثها: الأمة : التي تصل إلى أم الدماغ، وهي الجلدة التي فيها الدماغ، والجائفة : الجراحة التي وصلت إلى الجوف. (وفي جائفة نفذت ثلثاها ) ؛ لأنها بمنزلة الجائفتين. [1] قوله : بأن يسبر ؛ من سبرت الجرح أسبر: إذا نظرت ما غوره، والمسبار : بالكسر ما يقدر به قدر غور الجرح ، وهو بالفارسية : فتيله زخم. [2] قوله: نصف عشر الدية... الخ ؛ أخرج عبد الرزاق في «مصنّفه» عن زيد بن ثابته قال : «في الموضحة خمس ، وفي الهاشمة عشر، وفي النقلة خمس عشرة، وفي المأمومة ثلث الدية. كذا في «شرح النقاية». [3] قوله : والآمة ؛ لما روى في كتاب عمرو بن حزم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «في المأمومة ثلث الدِّيَة ، وفي الجائفة ثلث، وفي المنقلة خمس عشرة " [4] قوله : وفي جائفة ... الخ ؛ لما روى عن أبي بكر له أنه حكم في جائفة نفذت إلى الجانب الآخر بثلثي الدِّية ؛ ولأنها إذا نفذت نزلت منزلة جائفتين، إحداهما من جانب البطن، والأخرى من جانب الظهر، وفي كلِّ جائفة ثلث الدية ، فلذا وجب في النافذة ثلثا الدية. هكذا في «الهداية».
And in it (the khidash) is half a tenth of the blood money. In the hashimah is one tenth, and in the munqilah: one tenth and half a tenth, and in the ammah and jaifah is one third, and in a penetrating jaifah is two thirds. By probing its depth with a probe, then taking an iron of that measure and cutting accordingly. The wound that exposes the bone is the mudihah: it shows the bone. The khidash is half a tenth. The hashimah is one tenth: it breaks the bone. The munqilah is one tenth and half a tenth: it shifts the bone after breaking. The ammah and jaifah: one third. The ammah reaches the membrane of the brain. The jaifah reaches the belly. A penetrating jaifah is two thirds because it is like two jaifahs. Commentary: [1] His saying 'by probing' means measuring the depth. [2] His saying 'half a tenth' etc.: Abd al-Razzaq narrated from Zaid ibn Thabit: 'In the mudihah five, in the hashimah ten, in the munqilah fifteen, in the ma'mumah one third of the diyat.' [3] His saying 'the ammah' because it is narrated in the book of Amr ibn Hazm that the Prophet said: 'In the ma'mumah one third, in the jaifah one third, in the munqilah fifteen.' [4] His saying 'and in a penetrating jaifah' because it is narrated that Abu Bakr ruled in a penetrating jaifah to the other side with two thirds, and because it is like two jaifahs, one from the front and one from the back, each being one third, so the penetrating one requires two thirds. This is in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hukm with dalil) stating the fixed diyat amounts for specific head wounds. The matn (text) enumerates: khidash (scratch) gets half a tenth of the full diyat (i.e., 1/20), hashimah (bone-breaking) gets one tenth (1/10), munqilah (bone-displacing) gets one tenth plus half a tenth (3/20), ammah (membrane-reaching) and jaifah (belly-reaching) each get one third (1/3), and a penetrating jaifah (going through the body) gets two thirds (2/3). The commentator explains each wound, then provides proofs: a hadith from Zaid ibn Thabit via Abd al-Razzaq giving similar amounts, a hadith from the book of Amr ibn Hazm where the Prophet specifies for ma'mumah (same as ammah) and jaifah, and a narration that Abu Bakr ruled two thirds for a penetrating jaifah. He also uses qiyas: a penetrating jaifah is like two jaifahs (one in front, one in back), so it deserves double the single jaifah. The Hanafi school follows these fixed amounts as Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الخطا (al-khidash): A wound that scratches the skin without exposing bone.
الهاشمة (al-hāshimah): A wound that breaks the bone.
المنقلة (al-munqilah): A wound that displaces the bone after breaking it.
الآمة (al-āmmah): A wound that reaches the membrane covering the brain (Umm al-dimagh).
الجائفة (al-jā’ifah): A wound that reaches the internal body cavity (belly).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the diyat for a penetrating jaifah (wound going through the body)?
A) Half a tenth
B) One third
C) Two thirds
D) Full diyat

Key Takeaway
The fixed diyat fractions for these six head wounds are based on explicit Sunnah and qiyas, providing a clear legal scale for severity.

Wounds Requiring Hukumat 'Adl: Definitions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Definition and ruling for six head wounds with no fixed diyat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six head wounds with no fixed diyat, state their ruling (hukumat 'adl), and explain the verbal difference between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the order of mutalahimah and badi'ah.

Core Idea
Six head wounds have no fixed diyat — only arbitration, and the order dispute is purely verbal.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines six specific head wounds that do not fall under the fixed diyat amounts prescribed in the Sunnah. Though they differ in severity, all six require equally a 'hukumat 'adl' — arbitration by a just judge. The commentator also resolves an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the order of two of them, demonstrating that a difference in definition does not always affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six wounds
List them: al-harisah, al-dami'ah, al-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, and al-simhaq. Note their spellings and literal meanings as given in the commentary.
2
State the common ruling
All six wounds have no fixed diyat (muqaddar). The compensation is determined by hukumat 'adl (arbitration). This is the explicit ruling in the matn.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad places mutalahimah before badi'ah (shows flesh without cutting), while Abu Yusuf places it after badi'ah (goes into the flesh). The commentator notes this is a verbal difference only, not affecting the ruling.
The dispute is between two giants of the Hanafi school; both agree on the ruling.
4
Understand the scope
These wounds are specific to the face and head (wujuh wa ru'us). If similar wounds occur on other limbs, they are called jarahah and also have no fixed diyat — only hukumat 'adl.
The fixed diyat amounts (muqaddaraat) apply only to the head and face due to the meaning of disfigurement (shayn).
5
Note the exclusion of damighah
The damighah (reaches the brain) is not mentioned because it is rare and typically results from a separate injury, not a single wound with its own ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحارصة، والدامعة، والدامية، والباضعة، والمتلاحمة والسمحاق حكومة عدل

[1] قوله : الحارصة ؛ بالحاء والراء والصاد الغير المعجمات. والدامعة بالدال والعين المهملتين. والدامية : بالدال المهملة والياء المثناة التحتانية. والباضعة : بالباء الموحدة والضاد المعجمة والعين المهملة. والسمحاق : بكسر السين والحاء المهملتين على وزون الحرمان، وقد مر . قبل، وفسرها الشارح . [2] قوله : والمتلاحمة ؛ قال في «الهداية» : وعن محمد الله أنه جعل المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة، وقال هي التي يتلاحم فيها الدم ويسود ، وما ذكره الشارح بقوله : ما يأخذ في اللحم مروي عن أبي يوسف له ، وهذا الاختلاف اختلافُ عبارة، لا يؤثر في اختلاف الحكم. فمحمد الله ذهب إلى أنّ المتلاحمة مشتقة من التحم الساقان : إذا اتصل أحدهما بالآخر، فالمتلاحمة ما يُظهِرُ اللحم ولا يقطع ، والباضعة بعدها ؛ لأنها تقطعه. [3] قوله : والسمحاق ؛ إلى هنا بيان الشجاج العشرة ، وبعد هذا شجة أخرى تسمى الدامغة بالدال المهملة والغين المعجمة: وهي التي تصل إلى الدماغ، وإنما لم يذكرها ؛ لأنها تقع مثلاً في الغالب، لا جناية مقتصرة مفردة بحكم على حدة، ثمّ هذه الشجاج تختص بالوجه والرأس لغة وما كان في غير الوجه والرأس يسمى جراحة. والحكم مرتب على الحقيقة في الصحيح، حتى لو تحققت في غيرهما نحو الساق واليد لا يكون لها أرش مقدر، وإنّما تجب حكومة العدل ؛ لأنّ التقدير بالتوصيف، وهو إنّما ورد فيما اختص بهما ؛ ولأنه إنّما ورد الحكم فيها لمعنى الشين الذي يلحقه ببقاء أثر الجراحة، والشين يختص بما يظهر منها في الغالب، وهو العضوان هذان لا سواهما. أما اللحيان فقد قيل : ليسا من الوجه، وهو قول مالك الله حتى لو وُجد فيهما ما
And al-harisah, al-dami'ah, al-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, and al-simhaq: arbitration (hukumat 'adl).

[1] His saying: al-harisah: with ha, ra, and sad. Al-dami'ah: with dal and 'ayn. Al-damiyah: with dal and ya. Al-badi'ah: with ba and dad and 'ayn. Al-simhaq: with kasr of sin and ha, like hurman. [2] His saying: al-mutalahimah: in al-Hidayah, from Muhammad that he placed mutalahimah before badi'ah, and said it is where blood clots and turns black. What the commentator said 'what takes in the flesh' is narrated from Abu Yusuf. This difference is in wording, not affecting the ruling. Muhammad derived mutalahimah from iltiham (joining), meaning it shows the flesh without cutting, and badi'ah comes after because it cuts. [3] His saying: al-simhaq; up to here is explanation of the ten head wounds. After this is another wound called damighah which reaches the brain, but it is not mentioned because it is rare. These wounds are linguistically specific to the face and head; wounds elsewhere are called jarahah. The ruling depends on reality: if they occur elsewhere like leg or hand, there is no fixed amount, only arbitration, because the fixed amounts are based on the description (shayn) which is specific to these parts. As for the jaws, some say they are not part of the face, according to Malik.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling (ta'rif and hukm) for six types of head wounds that have no fixed diyat (muqaddar) but require hukumat 'adl (arbitration by a fair judge). The matn lists: harisah (scratcher, skin-deep), dami'ah (tearer, causing tears?), damiyah (bleeder), badi'ah (cutter through skin), mutalahimah (flesh-deep?), and simhaq (scalp-deep, reaching the membrane covering the skull). The commentator clarifies spellings and provides definitions. He then highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the order of mutalahimah and badi'ah. Muhammad places mutalahimah before badi'ah, defining it as a wound that shows flesh but does not cut it, while badi'ah cuts the flesh. Abu Yusuf defines mutalahimah as a wound that goes into the flesh. The commentator notes this is a verbal difference without practical effect on the ruling—both require arbitration. The passage further explains that these ten types (including damighah not mentioned) apply only to the face and head; if similar wounds occur on other limbs, they have no fixed amount and require arbitration. The ruling is based on the meaning of disfigurement (shayn) which is most visible on the head and face. The Hanafi position is that the jaws (lahan) are not part of the face according to some views.

Key Terms
الحارصة (al-ḥāriṣah): A wound that scratches the skin superficially.
الدامعة (al-dāmi‘ah): A wound that causes tears (or blood?) – likely a superficial tear.
الدامية (al-dāmiyah): A wound that draws blood.
الباضعة (al-bāḍi‘ah): A wound that cuts through the skin into the flesh.
المتلاحمة (al-mutalāḥimah): A wound that shows flesh but does not cut it, according to Muhammad; according to Abu Yusuf, it goes into the flesh.
السمحاق (al-simḥāq): A wound that reaches the membrane covering the skull (scalp).
حكومة عدل (ḥukūmat ‘adl): Arbitration by qualified judges to estimate compensation when no fixed amount exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fixed diyat of one tenth
B) Fixed diyat of one third
C) Hukumat 'adl (arbitration)
D) No compensation

Key Takeaway
Six head wounds (harisah to simhaq) all require hukumat 'adl, and the verbal dispute on the order of mutalahimah and badi'ah does not affect the ruling.

Diyat — Blood Money

The Five Scalp Wounds and Hukumat 'Adl: Definitions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Defines five head wounds and states common ruling of equitable assessment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define each of the five scalp wounds and state that they all require hukumat 'adl, not fixed diyah.

Core Idea
Five scalp wounds have no fixed compensation; only an equitable assessment (hukumat 'adl) is wajib.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every head wound triggers a fixed amount of blood money (diyah). For minor wounds that do not reach the bone—such as a scratch or a cut that exposes flesh—the law prescribes an equitable assessment called hukumat 'adl. This lesson defines these five wounds and explains how the compensation is calculated, while also noting a minor intra-Hanafi difference in terminology that does not affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five wounds and their definitions
From the text, list the five wounds: al-harisah (scratch without bleeding), al-dami'ah (bleeding like tears), al-dammiyah (flowing blood), al-badi'ah (cutting the skin), al-mutalahimah (entering the flesh), and al-simhaq (reaching the membrane over the skull). Note the specific linguistic descriptions.
The sixth wound, al-damighah (reaching the brain), is mentioned but excluded from this set because it is rare and not a separate ruling.
2
Recognize the common ruling: hukumat 'adl
All five wounds share the same legal consequence: hukumat 'adl (equitable assessment). No fixed diyah is prescribed for them. Understand why: the fixed amounts apply only to deeper wounds that cause disfigurement on the face/head.
Hukumat 'adl is wajib (obligatory) because the compensation is not fixed by text but determined by assessment.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on mutalahimah
Imam Muhammad placed mutalahimah before badi'ah and defined it as a wound that shows flesh without cutting. Abu Yusuf defined it as entering the flesh (more than badi'ah). The author states this is a difference of wording only, not affecting the ruling.
Both accept hukumat 'adl; the dispute has no practical consequence (thamarah).
4
Explain the method of hukumat 'adl
The injured person is hypothetically valued as a slave without the wound and with the wound. The difference in value is taken as a proportion of the full diyah (10,000 dirhams for a free man). For example, if the value drops by one-tenth, then one-tenth of the diyah is due.
This method is attributed to al-Tahawi and adopted by Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a wound on the head that is classified as al-badi'ah. Since it is one of the five, the compensation is hukumat 'adl. Estimate the difference in value (e.g., assume a slave's value without the wound is 1000, with the wound is 950; difference = 50, which is 5% of 1000; pay 5% of 10,000 = 500 dirhams).
The wound must be on the face or head; if on another limb, it is a jarah and also gets hukumat 'adl (no fixed arsh).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

التحتانية. والباضعة بالباء الموحدة والضاد المعجمة والعين المهملة. والمتلاحمة قال في الهداية: وعن محمد أنه جعل المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة، وقال هي التي يتلاحم فيها الدم ويسود، وما ذكره الشارح بقوله: ما يأخذ The Book of Blood Money: Its Types and Rulings. (Al-harisah, al-dami'ah, al-dammiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, and al-simhaq: equitable assessment.) He said: al-harisah: with ha, ra, and sad without dots. Al-dami'ah: with dal and 'ayn without dots. Al-dammiyah: with dal and ya under. Al-badi'ah: with ba, dad, and 'ayn. Al-mutalahimah: It is said in Al-Hidayah: From Muhammad that he placed mutalahimah before badi'ah, and said it is where blood coagulates and blackens, and what the commentator mentioned by his saying 'what enters the flesh' is narrated from Abu Yusuf, and this difference is a difference of expression, not affecting the ruling. Muhammad holds that mutalahimah is derived from 'iltaham al-saqan' when one leg connects with the other, so mutalahimah is what shows the flesh but does not cut, and badi'ah is after it because it cuts. Al-simhaq: Up to here is the explanation of the ten head wounds, and after this there is another wound called al-damighah: which reaches the brain, and he did not mention it because it occurs rarely, not a single confined injury with an independent ruling. Then these head wounds are specific to the face and head linguistically, and what is elsewhere is called a wound. The ruling is based on the reality according to the correct view, such that if they occur elsewhere like the shin or hand, there is no fixed compensation, rather equitable assessment is obligatory because the fixed estimation is by description, and that is only transmitted for what is specific to them; and because the ruling was transmitted for them due to the meaning of disfigurement that follows from the trace of the wound, and disfigurement is specific to what appears from them mostly, and these two limbs, not others. As for the jaws, it has been said they are not from the face, and that is the saying of Malik, such that if what occurs on the face occurs on them, then he assesses a slave without this trace, then with it, and the difference between the two values from the blood money. That is, what scratches the skin: that is, scratches it, and blood appears but does not flow like tears from the eye, and what makes blood flow, and what cuts the skin: that is, cuts it, and what enters the flesh, and what reaches the simhaq: that is, a thin skin between the flesh and the skull. Then he explained equitable assessment by his saying: He assesses a slave without this trace, then with it, and the difference between the two values from the blood money is it; 'huwa' refers to the amount of difference, and 'hiya' refers to equitable assessment. So it is assumed that this free person is a slave, and his value without this trace is a thousand dirhams, and with this trace nine hundred dirhams, so the difference between them is one hundred dirhams, which is a tenth of a thousand, so this difference is taken from the blood money which is ten thousand dirhams, so its tenth is one thousand dirhams, which is a tenth of the blood money. This is because the face is derived from facing, and there is no facing for the observer, except that according to us they are part of the face due to their connection without a gap, and the meaning of facing may also be realized. Thus in Al-Hidayah. His saying: i.e. what scratches... etc.; this is the beginning of the explanation of what is in the text from his saying: al-harisah to al-simhaq, and it is arranged according to the order of the wording. His saying: 'and what enters the flesh'; i.e. goes into the flesh more than the badi'ah goes, this is the apparent narration, so based on this, applying mutalahimah to what cuts the flesh is only by considering what it leads to or optimism. His saying: 'he assesses a slave'; this is the saying of al-Tahawi, and Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani adopted it. Its summary: that he assesses a slave without this trace, and assesses with this trace, then looks at the difference between the two values; if it is half a tenth of the value, half a tenth of the blood money is obligatory; if a quarter of a tenth, then a quarter of a tenth of the blood money. Thus in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah deals with the types of head wounds (shijaj) that do not reach the bone. The author defines five specific wounds: al-harisah (a scratch that appears but does not bleed), al-dami'ah (bleeds like tears), al-dammiyah (blood flows), al-badi'ah (cuts the skin), al-mutalahimah (enters the flesh), and al-simhaq (reaches the thin membrane over the skull). For all these, the compensation is not a fixed amount but rather hukumat 'adl (equitable assessment). The text then discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the order of mutalahimah and badi'ah. Imam Muhammad places mutalahimah before badi'ah, understanding it as a wound that exposes flesh without cutting it, while Abu Yusuf defines it as entering the flesh. The author notes that this difference is merely in expression and does not affect the ruling. The commentary further explains that these rulings are specific to the face and head; if such wounds occur elsewhere on the body, they are considered jarah (wounds) and have no fixed compensation—only hukumat 'adl applies. The method of hukumat 'adl is then explained: the injured person is hypothetically evaluated as a slave with and without the wound; the difference in value is taken as a proportion of the full diyah (10,000 dirhams for a free man). For example, if the difference is one-tenth of the slave's value, one-tenth of the diyah is payable. This principle is based on preserving the appearance (shayn) of the face and head. The passage also clarifies that the jaws (lahyan) are considered part of the face according to the Hanafi school, contrary to Malik's view, because they are connected directly to the face and can also be subject to facial disfigurement.

Key Terms
الحارصة (al-harisah): The scratch that scratches the skin without causing bleeding.
الدامعة (al-dami'ah): A wound that produces blood like tears but does not flow.
الدامية (al-dammiyah): A wound from which blood flows.
الباضعة (al-badi'ah): A wound that cuts the skin.
المتلاحمة (al-mutalahimah): A wound that goes into the flesh but does not reach the bone.
السمحاق (al-simhaq): A wound that reaches the thin membrane (periosteum) between the flesh and the skull.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): An equitable assessment of compensation by evaluating the difference in value of a person with and without the wound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the compensation for al-harisah (the scratch) on the head?
A) A fixed one-tenth of the diyah
B) Hukumat 'adl (equitable assessment)
C) Qisas (retaliation)
D) No compensation

Key Takeaway
Five non‑bone‑deep scalp wounds (al‑harisah, al‑dami'ah, al‑dammiyah, al‑badi'ah, al‑mutalahimah, al‑simhaq) are all compensated by hukumat 'adl, an equitable assessment of the loss in value, not by a fixed diyah.

Classification of Head Wounds and Hukumat Adl
Defines five head wound types and explains hukumat adl calculation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five types of head wounds in the Hanafi school and apply the method of hukumat adl to calculate compensation for wounds without a fixed diya.

Core Idea
For head wounds without fixed diya, compensation is proportional to the loss of value, illustrated by hukumat adl.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary defines five types of head wounds and explains the calculation of discretionary compensation (hukumat adl). Understanding wound classification is essential for proper application of diya rules, and the proportional valuation method shows the school's commitment to individualized justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five wound types
List the five types in order: al-harisa (scratch, blood appears but not flow), al-dami'a (blood flows), al-badi'a (cuts skin), al-mutalahima (penetrates flesh), al-simhaq (reaches thin membrane before bone).
Al-mudiha (reaches bone) has a fixed diya and is not covered here.
2
Understand when hukumat adl applies
Hukumat adl applies to wounds without a fixed diya. The method is an estimation based on the loss of value.
Fixed wounds like mudiha have prescribed amounts; this method fills the gap.
3
Learn the valuation method
Assume the victim is a slave. Calculate value without injury and with injury. The proportional difference (out of the hypothetical slave value) is taken from the diya (10,000 dirhams).
Example: difference 100 out of 1000 → 1/10 of diya = 1000 dirhams.
4
Note the inclusion of face parts
Eyebrows and nose are part of the face for diya calculations due to direct connection.
Al-Hidayah states this is the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقوم عبداً بلا هذا الأثر، ثمّ معه، فقدرُ التّفاوت بين القيمتين من الدِّيَة . أي ما يحرص الجلد : أي يخدشُهُ ، ويظهرُ الدَّمَ ولا يسيلُهُ كالدَّمع من العين ) وما يسيل الدم، وما يبضع الجلد : أي يقطعه، وما يأخذ في اللحم، وما يصلُ إلى السمحاق، أي: جلدة رقيقة بين اللحم وعظم الرأس. ثم فسر حكومة العدل بقوله : (فيقوم عبدا ) بلا هذا الأثر، ثم معه ، فقدرُ التفاوت بين القيمتين من الدية هو هي) ، هو ؛ يرجع إلى قدر التفاوت. وهي ؛ ترجع إلى حكومة العدل، فيفرضُ أنَّ هذا الحرّ عبد، وقيمته بلا هذا الأثر ألفُ درهم، ومع هذا الأثر تسعمئة درهم ، فالتفاوت بينهما مئة . الألف، فيؤخذ هذا التفاوتُ من الدِّيَةِ وهى عشرة آلاف درهم، فعشرها ألف درهم، وهو عشر الدية أو أرش مفسد لا يجب المقدر ؛ وهذا لأنّ الوجه مشتق من المواجهة، ولا مواجهة للناظر فيهما إلا أن عندنا هما من الوجه لاتصالهما به من غير فاصلة، وقد يتحقق فيه معنى المواجهة أيضاً. هكذا في «الهداية». وبه يفتى
He assesses [the victim] as a slave without this effect, then with it, and the difference between the two values [is taken] from the diya. That is, what cuts the skin, i.e., scratches it, and makes blood appear but does not cause it to flow like tears from the eye; and what makes blood flow; and what cuts the skin, i.e., cuts it; and what goes into the flesh; and what reaches the simhaq, i.e., a thin skin between the flesh and the skull. Then he explained hukumat adl by his statement: 'He assesses as a slave without this effect, then with it, and the difference between the two values from the diya is it.' 'It' refers to the amount of difference, and 'it' refers to hukumat adl. It is assumed that this free person is a slave, and his value without this effect is a thousand dirhams, and with this effect nine hundred dirhams, so the difference between them is a hundred [dirhams] out of a thousand. So this difference is taken from the diya, which is ten thousand dirhams, and its tenth is a thousand dirhams. This is the tenth of the diya or a specified amount, not estimated. And this is because the face is derived from facing, and there is no facing for the viewer regarding them except that in our school they are from the face due to their connection to it without a separator, and the meaning of facing may also be realized in them. Thus in al-Hidayah. And according to this is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the classification of head wounds and the method of calculating compensation (hukumat adl) for wounds that do not have a fixed diya. The author first defines five types of wounds in order of severity: (1) al-harisa – scratches the skin and makes blood appear but not flow, like tears; (2) al-dami'a – causes blood to flow; (3) al-badi'a – cuts the skin; (4) al-mutalahima – penetrates into the flesh; (5) al-simhaq – reaches the thin membrane between flesh and skull. Then the text explains hukumat adl: the victim is hypothetically valued as a slave without the wound, then with the wound; the difference between these two values is taken as a proportion of the full diya (10,000 dirhams). For example, if the difference is 100 out of 1000, then one-tenth of the diya is due. This method applies to wounds where no fixed amount is prescribed. The passage also clarifies that the eyebrows and nose are considered part of the face for legal purposes, as they are connected without a gap. The commentary cites al-Hidayah and states that this is the fatwa.

Key Terms
حكومة العدل (hukumat adl): Estimation of compensation by assessing the victim as a slave without and with the injury, taking the difference proportionally from the diya.
الحارصة (al-harisa): A wound that scratches the skin, makes blood appear but does not flow.
السمحاق (al-simhaq): A thin membrane between the flesh and the skull; a wound reaching it is one degree shallower than al-mudiha.
الوجه (al-wajh): The face, derived from 'facing'; in legal context includes eyebrows and nose due to direct connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 1/4 of diya
B) 1/2 of diya
C) 3/4 of diya
D) 1/10 of diya

Key Takeaway
Hukumat adl ensures proportional compensation based on the actual loss caused by the injury.

Diya for Fingers, Hand, and Forearm (Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Forearm)
Sets fixed diya for fingers and explains rules for combined amputations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for diya in combined amputations of fingers/hand/forearm and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the forearm.

Core Idea
Fingers and palm together give half diya; the forearm is not a follow-up and requires hukumat adl.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the compensation (diya) for amputation of the hand and varying degrees of the arm. It establishes fixed amounts for fingers and hand based on prophetic hadith, and addresses a disagreement among Hanafi jurists on whether the forearm is a 'follow-up' to the hand.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish fixed diya from proportional compensation
Fixed diya applies to body parts with a prescribed amount; hukumat adl is assessed proportionally for parts without a fixed share.
Hukumat adl is based on loss of value, not a fixed sum.
2
Memorize the fixed diya for fingers and hand
Each finger has one-tenth diya (10 camels), so five fingers = half diya (50 camels). The palm is a follow-up, so amputation of fingers with or without palm results in half diya.
Hadith: 'In each finger, ten camels' and 'In the two hands, the full diya; in one hand, half diya.'
3
Understand the principle of taba' (follow-up)
A body part that is functionally dependent on another does not receive independent diya; its compensation is included in the main part's diya.
The palm is taba' for fingers because grasping is by them.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the forearm
The main Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn) holds that the forearm is not taba' of the hand, so extra compensation via hukumat adl is due. A narration from Abu Yusuf may consider it taba', giving no extra compensation.
The relied-upon view (mukhtar al-tarafayn) is that the forearm is not a follow-up.
5
Apply the ruling to combined amputations
For amputation of fingers/hand with half forearm: half diya for the hand (fingers + palm) plus hukumat adl for the forearm portion.
Example: hand + half forearm = 5000 dirhams fixed + hukumat adl for forearm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي أصابع يد بلا كف ومعها نصفُ الدِّية، ومع نصف الساعدِ نصفُ دية وحكومة عدل، فإنَّ الذراع ليست تبعا ، وفي رواية عن أبي يوسف, [١]قوله : إنه ينظر ... الخ؛ بيان هذا أنّ هذه الشجّة لو كانت باضعة مثلا ، فإنّه ينظر كم مقدار الباضعة من الموضحة ، فإن كان مقدارها ثلث الموضحة وجب ثلث أرش الموضحة، وإن كان ربعها يجب ربع أرشها ، وإن كان ثلاثة أرباعها ، يجب ثلاثة أرباع أرشها؛ لأنّ ما لا نص فيه يردُّ إلى المنصوص عليه، قال شيخ الاسلام : هذا هو الأصح. كذا في «الذخيرة». [٢]قوله: نصف الدية ؛ لأنّ في كلّ إصبع عشر الدية ؛ لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «في كل إصبع عشر من الإبل» ، فإن قطعها مع الكف ففيه أيضا نصف الدية ؛ لقوله : «وفي اليدين الدية»، وفي إحداهما نصف الدية ؛ ولأنّ الكفّ تبع للأصابع ؛ لأن البطش بها. And for the fingers of a hand without or with the palm, half the diya; and with half the forearm, half diya and hukumat adl; for the upper arm is not a follow-up. And in a narration from Abu Yusuf... [Footnote 1: He said: 'It is considered...' explanation that if this wound were, for example, a badi'a, then it is seen how much of the badi'a is from the mudiha, if it is one-third, then one-third of the arsh of mudiha is due, etc., because what has no text is referred to the textual. Shaykh al-Islam said this is the most correct.] [Footnote 2: He said: Half diya because in each finger is one-tenth diya due to the Prophet's saying: 'In each finger is ten camels.' If cut with the palm, also half diya due to his saying: 'In the two hands is the diya,' and in one hand half diya; and because the palm is a follow-up of the fingers, for grasping is by them.] [Footnote 3: He said: And with half the forearm... explanation that if cut with half the forearm, then for the fingers and palm is half diya, and for the increase is hukumat adl; this is the chosen view of both parties, and a narration from Abu Yusuf. In another narration from him it is what the sharh said because of his statement: 'What exceeds the fingers of the hand...' And for them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The hand is an organ for grasping, and grasping is related to the palm and fingers, not the forearm, so they did not make the forearm a follow-up in liability; and because there is no way for it to be a follow-up of the fingers, for there is a complete organ between them, nor to be a follow-up of the palm, for the palm is a follow-up of the fingers.]
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the chapter of diyat provides the rulings for amputation of fingers, hand, and forearm. The main ruling is clear: for five fingers, whether cut with or without the palm, half the diya (5000 dirhams) is due. This is based on hadith: each finger has ten camels (one-tenth diya total for five), and the hand (palm) is considered a follow-up to the fingers. When half the forearm is cut along with the hand, the hand (fingers and palm) gets half diya, and the extra forearm portion receives hukumat adl (proportional compensation). The forearm is not considered a follow-up of the hand because it is a separate organ not directly involved in grasping. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: a narration from Abu Yusuf (and possibly a different view) is mentioned. The relied-upon view is that the forearm is not a follow-up. The commentary also includes a general principle: for wounds without fixed diya (like a badi'a relative to mudiha), compensation is proportional to the closest analogous fixed wound. This is attributed to Shaykh al-Islam.

Key Terms
الدية (al-diya): Blood money, full compensation for homicide or loss of a limb; 10,000 dirhams or 100 camels.
تبع (taba'): Follow-up; a body part that is considered legally dependent on another, not deserving separate diya.
حكومة عدل (hukumat adl): Proportional compensation assessed by loss of value, not a fixed amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Full diya for the hand and half diya for the forearm
B) Half diya for the hand and hukumat adl for the forearm
C) Full diya for the entire arm
D) Half diya for both

Key Takeaway
Fingers and palm together give half diya; the forearm is not a follow-up and requires hukumat adl.

Diyat for Five Fingers and the Ruling of the Forearm
Intra-Hanafi dispute on subordination of forearm in diyat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyat for the hand and forearm and apply the rule of subordination to determine when hukumat al-adl is required.

Core Idea
The forearm is not subordinate to the hand in diyat; half diya covers fingers and palm, but the forearm requires separate equitable compensation.

This lesson addresses a specific dispute within the Hanafi school: whether the forearm (saa'id) is subordinate to the hand for purposes of diyat. The relied-upon view is that it is not, requiring two separate compensations: half diya for the hand and hukumat al-adl for the forearm. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct application in civil liability cases.

Guided Path
1
State the fixed rule for five fingers and hand
Recall that the diya for five fingers is half the full diya, whether cut with the palm or not, because the palm is subordinate to the fingers. This is based on the hadith 'In each finger ten camels' and 'In the two hands is the full diya'.
The half diya for the hand includes fingers and palm.
2
Identify the disputed point: subordination of the forearm
The main question is whether the forearm (saa'id) is subordinate to the hand in liability. If subordinate, half diya covers the entire cut including the forearm; if not, hukumat al-adl is due for the forearm.
The text explicitly states 'the forearm is not subordinate' but notes a narration from Abu Yusuf suggesting otherwise.
3
Present the opposing narration
There is a narration from Abu Yusuf that considers the forearm subordinate, and another from him equating the hand to the entire arm to the shoulder. However, these are not the relied-upon views.
The passage says 'wa bihi yufta' (and with it is given fatwa) for the non-subordinate view.
4
Analyse the argument of the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani)
The Two argue that the hand is a grasping instrument (batash), which involves the palm and fingers, not the forearm. Thus, the forearm is a separate complete organ and not subordinate to the fingers or palm.
Their reasoning is that grasping does not require the forearm, so it cannot be a subordinate part.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi view and its practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that the forearm is not subordinate. Therefore, if the hand is cut with part of the forearm, half diya is due for the hand (fingers and palm) and hukumat al-adl for the forearm. The practical consequence is additional compensation.
This is the view given fatwa, as indicated by 'wa bihi yufta'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

درهم، فهو حكومة العدل، وبه يفتى)، احتراز عما قال الكَرْ. : إنه ينظر) مقدار هذه الشَّجَّةِ من الموضحة فيجب بقدرِ ذلك من نصف عشر الدية. (وفي أصابع يد بلا كف ومعها نَصفُ الدِّيَة) : أي في خمس أصابع نصفُ الدِّيةِ سواء قطعها مع الكف أو بدونها ، فإنَّ الكف تابع لها ، (ومع نصف الساعد نصف دية وحكومة عدل، فإنَّ الذراع ليست تبعا ، وفي رواية عن أبي يوسف،

[٣] قوله : ومع نصف الساعد...الخ ؛ أي إن قطعَ لها مع نصف الساعد، ففي الأصابع والكف نصف الدية، وفي الزيادة حكومة عدل، وهو مختار الطرفين، ورواية عن أبي يوسف الله ، وفي رواية أخرى عنه ما قاله الشارح له لقوله : إن ما زاد على أصابع اليد... الخ.

ولهما : إن اليد آلة باطشة، والبطش يتعلّق بالكف والأصابع دون الذراع فلم يجعل الذراع تبعاً في حق التضمين ؛ ولأنه لا وجة إلى أن يكون تبعاً للأصابع ؛ لأن بينهما عضواً كاملاً ، ولا وجه إلى أن يكون تبعاً للكف ؛ لأنّ الكف تابع للأصابع، ولا
And with it is given fatwa. And for fingers of a hand without a palm and with it, half of the diya. And with half of the forearm, half diya and hukumat al-adl of a dirham, so it is hukumat al-adl, and with it is given fatwa, a precaution from what al-Kar said: Indeed, one looks at the amount of this wound relative to the mawdhihah, then an amount proportional to that from half of one-tenth of the diya is obligatory. (And for fingers of a hand without a palm and with it is half of the diya): meaning for five fingers is half the diya, whether he cut them with the palm or without it, for the palm is subordinate to them. (And with half the forearm, half diya and hukumat al-adl, for the forearm is not subordinate, and in a narration from Abu Yusuf...) [Footnote 1] His statement: 'Indeed, he looks... etc.' Explanation: If this wound were, for example, a baadi'ah, then one looks at how much the baadi'ah is relative to the mawdhihah. If it is one-third, then one-third of the arsh of mawdhihah is obligatory; if one-quarter, one-quarter; if three-quarters, three-quarters, because what has no fixed text is referred to the textually specified. Shaykh al-Islam said: This is the most correct. As in al-Dhakhirah. [Footnote 2] His statement: 'Half the diya' because in each finger is one-tenth of the diya, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'In each finger ten camels.' If he cuts them with the palm, then also half the diya, due to his saying: 'In the two hands is the diya, and in one of them is half the diya,' and because the palm is subordinate to the fingers, for grasping is done with them. [Footnote 3] His statement: 'And with half the forearm... etc.' meaning if he cuts it with half the forearm, then for the fingers and palm is half the diya, and for the excess is hukumat al-adl, and this is the chosen view of both parties, and a narration from Abu Yusuf, and in another narration from him is what the sharh said, due to his statement: 'What is in excess of the fingers of the hand... etc.' And for the Two: The hand is a grasping instrument, and grasping is related to the palm and fingers, not the forearm, so the forearm is not made subordinate in respect of liability. Because there is no way for it to be subordinate to the fingers, as between them is a complete organ, and no way for it to be subordinate to the palm, because the palm is subordinate to the fingers, and no...
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the diyat (blood money) for injuries to the hand and forearm. The main ruling is that for five fingers, half the full diya is due, whether the palm is cut with them or not, because the palm is subordinate to the fingers. This is supported by the hadith: 'In each finger, ten camels' and 'In the two hands, the full diya.' The forearm, however, is not subordinate, so if it is cut along with the hand, half diya is due for the hand and hukumat al-adl (equitable compensation) for the forearm. There is a narration from Abu Yusuf that the forearm is subordinate, but the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that it is not. The Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the hand is a grasping tool, involving the palm and fingers, so the forearm is a separate organ. The passage also defines hukumat al-adl for wounds with no fixed compensation: they are proportional to the nearest specified wound, e.g., the mawdhihah (scalp wound exposing bone).

Key Terms
دية (dīyah): The fixed monetary compensation for homicide or injury, e.g., full diya for a hand is half of the diya for a life.
حكومة عدل (hukūmat al-adl): Equitable compensation estimated by a judge for injuries with no fixed arsh, proportionally to the nearest specified wound.
تبع (tābi'): Subordinate; in diyat, a body part that follows another in liability, so its diya is included in the primary part's diya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person's hand is cut off with half of the forearm, what is the diya according to the mufta bihi view?
A) Full diya for the hand plus hukumat for the forearm
B) Half diya for the hand plus hukumat for the forearm
C) Half diya for the hand and no extra for the forearm
D) Full diya for the whole limb

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi view: half diya for the hand (fingers+palm) and hukumat al-adl for the forearm, as the forearm is not subordinate.

Diyah for Fingers with Palm: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on priority of palm vs finger arsh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah for a palm with fingers attached and apply the principle of subordination (tabi').

Core Idea
When fingers are present, the palm is subordinate — Abu Hanifa gives only the finger's arsh; the Sahibayn compare and take the greater.

This passage presents a subtle disagreement within the Hanafi school on how to calculate the blood money (diyah) when a palm with attached fingers is severed. Abu Hanifa considers the palm subordinate to the fingers, so only the fixed compensation for the fingers is due. The Sahibayn argue that both the palm and the fingers have independent values, and the greater should be paid. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principle of tabi' in diyat and see how ikhtilaf drives legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the dispute
The case is a palm cut off with one or more fingers still attached. The dispute is whether the palm itself deserves separate compensation beyond the fingers' fixed arsh.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa: only the fingers' arsh is due (1/10 per finger, up to 3 fingers). The palm is subordinate (tabi') to the fingers, so no extra payment. His dalil: the Lawgiver fixed arsh only for fingers; the palm has no independent share when fingers are present.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): look at the hukuma of the palm and the arsh of the fingers; the greater amount is taken. The lesser is included in the greater. Their dalil: the palm has independent value when cut alone, so it should not be ignored.
4
Evaluate the consensus and the thamarah al-khilaf
For three fingers, there is consensus that only the fingers' arsh is due and nothing for the palm, because the majority (3 out of 5) takes the ruling of the whole. This supports Abu Hanifa's general principle. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): under Abu Hanifa, less is paid; under Sahibayn, more is paid if the palm's hukuma is higher.
The text also notes that the forearm is not subordinate, so a hukuma is required for it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كف فيها أصبع عشرها، وإن كانت أصبعان فخمسها، ولا شيء في الكف)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا : ينظر إلى إرش الكف والأصبع فيكون عليه الأكثر، ويدخل القليلُ في الكثير، وإن كانت ثلاثة أصابع يجب أرسُ الأصابع، ولا شيء في الكف بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ للأكثر حكم الكل، فاستتبعت الكف. تبع للتبع ؛ لأنه من حيث التبع لا يجب للكف شيء، ومن حيث أنه أصل الساعد وجب أن يجب له شيء فيؤدي إلى الوجوب وعدمه. لا ولما لم يكن الساعد تبعاً لا إلى الأصابع ولا إلى الكف وجب اعتباره أصلاً ؛ إذ وجه إلى إهداره ، ولم يرد من الشارع فيه شيء مقدّر فتجب فيه حكومة عدل، هكذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية».
And in a palm that has a finger, its tenth (of the finger's diyah), and if there are two fingers, its fifth, and nothing is due for the palm—this according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Consider the arsh of the palm and the finger, and the greater of the two is incumbent upon him, and the lesser is included in the greater. And if there are three fingers, the arsh of the fingers is obligatory, and nothing is due for the palm by consensus, because the majority has the ruling of the whole, so the palm follows. Subordinate to the subordinate: because from the perspective of subordination, nothing is due for the palm, and from the perspective that it is the origin of the forearm, it would be necessary that something be due for it, leading to both obligation and non-obligation. No. And since the forearm is not subordinate to either the fingers or the palm, it is necessary to consider it as an origin; because its value would otherwise be nullified, and nothing specific has been transmitted from the Lawgiver concerning it, so a fair assessment (hukuma) is required. Thus in some glosses of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage deals with the diyah (blood money) for cutting off a palm that still has fingers attached. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The rule is stated: if a palm has one finger, the arsh (fixed compensation) is one-tenth of the full diyah; if two fingers, two-tenths (fifth); and nothing is due for the palm itself. This is Abu Hanifa's position. He considers the fingers as the principal (asl) and the palm as subordinate (tabi'), so no separate compensation for the palm. The Sahibayn, however, argue that one should look at both the arsh of the palm (which would be a hukuma, i.e., assessed value) and the fixed arsh of the fingers, and the greater of the two is imposed; the lesser is included in the greater. For three fingers, there is consensus that only the fingers' arsh is due and nothing for the palm, because the majority (three out of five fingers) takes the ruling of the whole, making the palm entirely subordinate. The text then discusses the forearm: it is not subordinate to either the fingers or the palm, so it must be considered independently. Since no fixed amount is given by the Lawgiver for the forearm, a hukuma (fair assessment) is required. This explanation is drawn from glosses on al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Fixed compensation (blood money) for injuries to specific body parts, determined by the Lawgiver.
تبع (tabi'): Subordinate; a body part that follows another in compensation, i.e., no separate arsh.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): An assessed fair compensation when no fixed arsh is prescribed, determined by a judge or expert.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 1/10 of full diyah for the finger, and nothing for the palm
B) 1/10 for the finger plus a hukuma for the palm
C) Only a hukuma for the palm because it is the origin
D) Nothing because the finger is subordinate

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi diyat law, when fingers are present on the palm, the palm is subordinate and no separate arsh is due; the Sahibayn's contrary view is outweighed by Abu Hanifa's and the consensus for three fingers.

Diyah for Body Parts with Unknown Functionality: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on hukuma vs full diyah when health unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on compensation for an extra finger, child's eye, penis, and tongue when functionality is unknown, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the default of health.

Core Idea
When benefit is uncertain, Hanafis require hukuma, not full diyah; certainty is needed for fixed penalties.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute regarding diyah for body parts whose primary benefit is unknown. The Hanafi school insists on evidence of functionality before imposing full diyah, while the Shafi'i school presumes health as the default. Understanding this disagreement illuminates the Hanafi principle that financial obligations require certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested body parts
The passage lists an extra finger, a child's eye, his penis, and his tongue. For each, the primary benefit (sight, erection, speech) is unknown.
These parts have no fixed diyah in the texts; their value depends on function.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Hanafis hold that only a fair assessment (hukuma) is due, not the full diyah. This is because the essential benefit is not proven.
Hukuma is assessed by a qualified expert based on the part's potential value.
3
State the Shafi'i ruling and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i rules that full diyah is obligatory because the default (ghalib) condition of such organs is health and functionality.
The Shafi'i rely on the principle that the majority of cases indicate health.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis reject the default as a basis for imposing a fixed financial penalty. Certainty (yaqin) is required for fixed diyah amounts, not probability.
This reflects the Hanafi usul principle that obligations cannot be based on doubt.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi, the victim receives a variable assessed amount; under Shafi'i, a full fixed diyah (e.g., 100 camels for a limb).
The difference can be substantial in financial terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي أصبع زائدة، وعين صبي، وذكره ، ولسانه، لو لم يعلم الصحة بما دل على نظره، وتحرك ذكره، وكلامه حكومة عدل )، هذا عندنا)، وعند الشافعي يجب ديَّة كاملة ؛ لأنَّ الغالب الصحة ، أما إن عَلِمَ صحة هذه الأعضاء، فالواجب الدية الكاملة اتفاقاً.
And for an extra finger, a child's eye, his penis, and his tongue, if the health (of the eye, etc.) is not known by evidence of his sight, movement of his penis, or his speech, then a fair assessment (hukuma) is due—this according to us. According to al-Shafi'i, a full diyah is obligatory, because the default is health. If the health of these organs is known, then the full diyah is obligatory by consensus.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage addresses the compensation for injuries to certain body parts when their functionality is unknown. The Hanafi position (stated as 'our view') is that if we do not know whether a child's eye was sighted, whether his penis was erectile, or whether his tongue could speak, then only a hukuma (assessed compensation) is due, not the full diyah. This is because the primary purpose of these parts is benefit (manfa'a), and we cannot be certain of their usefulness. Imam al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, argues for the full diyah, presuming health as the default condition. However, if health is proven, then all schools agree on full diyah.

Key Terms
أصبع زائدة (isba' za'idah): An extra finger beyond the normal five; its diyah is not fixed.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Assessed compensation when no fixed amount is prescribed.
الغالب (al-ghalib): The predominant state; Shafi'i uses it to presume health.
