Inclusion of Mudihah Arsh in Full Diyah: Hanafi vs. Zufar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on absorption of wound arsh in diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on absorption of mudihah arsh into diyah and distinguish when it does and does not apply, citing the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufar.

Core Idea
Mudihah arsh is absorbed into diyah only when it causes total loss (mind or hair), not partial sensory loss.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about overlapping compensations for head wounds. The Hanafi school draws a distinction between injuries that cause a comprehensive loss (like insanity or total scalp hair loss) versus those that impair a specific sense (hearing, sight, speech). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly calculating blood money (diyah) in complex injury cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed question
The text asks: When a mudihah (head wound exposing bone) leads to loss of a major faculty, does the fixed arsh of the mudihah (5 camels) get included in the full diyah (100 camels) or must both be paid separately?
This is about 'dakhala fi' – legal absorption of a lesser sum into a greater.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafi school, absorption depends on the type of loss. For loss of mind (akhl al-'aql) or loss of scalp hair (sha'r ra's), the arsh is absorbed into the diyah. For loss of hearing, sight, or speech, the arsh is NOT absorbed; both are paid separately.
The text explicitly says 'dakhala' for 'mind or hair' and 'la' for senses.
3
Understand the Hanafi rationale
The distinction rests on severity: loss of mind renders all organs useless – the diyah is comprehensive. Hair loss is considered a head injury enhancement. Sensory losses are independent injuries each deserving their own arsh.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) – comparing the comprehensiveness of the loss.
4
Contrast with Zufar's position
Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl holds that the arsh of a mudihah is never absorbed into the diyah, regardless of the resulting loss. Both compensations always remain separate.
Zufar was a major Hanafi disciple; this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, under the Hanafi view, if a mudihah causes insanity, only the full diyah is due. Under Zufar, the diyah plus the arsh (5 camels extra) would be due, totalling 105 camels.
The mufta bihi position is the Hanafi view as stated in the text ('this according to us').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل أرسُ مُوضحة أذهبت عقلَهُ أو شعر رأسه في الدية (٣) ، وإن ذهب سمعه ، أو بصره ، أو نطقه لا (٣) ، هذا عندنا ، وعند زفره : لا يدخل
And the arsh of a head wound (mudihah) that caused the loss of his mind or the hair of his head enters into the full diyah. And if it caused the loss of his hearing, or his sight, or his speech, then it does not enter. This according to us. According to Zufar: it does not enter.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage discusses whether the fixed compensation (arsh) for a specific head wound (mudihah) is absorbed into the full diyah when that wound leads to a total loss of a major faculty. The Hanafi position (attributed to Abu Hanifa and the school) is that if the mudihah caused loss of intellect or hair loss, the arsh is included in the diyah, meaning only the full diyah is paid, not the arsh separately. This is because the loss of mind nullifies the benefit of all organs, and hair loss is considered part of the head. However, if the wound causes loss of hearing, sight, or speech, the arsh of the mudihah is NOT included; both compensations are due separately, because each of these faculties is a separate injury. Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl disagrees completely: according to him, the arsh of the mudihah is never included in the diyah; both are always separate.

Key Terms
موضحة (mudihah): A head wound that exposes the bone; its fixed arsh is 1/20 of diyah (5 camels).
دية (diyah): Full blood money for killing or loss of a major faculty (100 camels or equivalent).
دخل في (dakhala fi): Legal absorption: the lesser compensation is included in the greater.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, included
B) No, both are paid separately
C) Only if hearing is permanently lost
D) Only if the wound is severe

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that the arsh of a mudihah is absorbed into the diyah only when the resulting loss is comprehensive (mind or hair), not for independent sensory losses.

Compensation for Organs with Unknown Health: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on compensation for organs with unknown health

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation for organs with unknown health, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When an organ's function is unproven, Hanafis require a just assessment (hukumat 'adl), not a full fixed diyah.

Injuries to organs like a supernumerary finger, a child's eye, the penis, or the tongue raise a question: should compensation be fixed or assessed case-by-case? The Hanafi school argues that if the organ's health and function cannot be verified, the full arsh (fixed compensation) is not obligatory; instead, a just assessment (hukumat 'adl) is made by experts. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, holds that the default assumption is health, so full diyah is due. This lesson examines the evidence and the practical consequence of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the organs and the uncertainty
The text lists four organs: supernumerary finger, child's eye, penis, and tongue. For each, health and function are not conclusively known (e.g., child's eye sight, tongue's speech).
Health is known through visible indications like vision, movement, or speech; if absent, health remains uncertain.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
Hanafi: hukumat 'adl (just assessment) is wajib. Basis: these organs are for benefit (manfa'ah). Without proof of function, full arsh is not obligatory due to doubt. The mere outward appearance (zāhir) is insufficient to impose liability, unlike cosmetic organs (nose, ear) where beauty is the purpose and destruction is complete.
The text calls hukumat 'adl an 'honor for the human' because it individualizes compensation.
3
State the Shafi'i ruling and its basis
Shafi'i: full diyah is obligatory because the norm (ghalabah) is that organs are healthy. The default presumption supports fixed compensation.
This is a methodological difference: Shafi'i relies on probability (ghalabah) as legal proof; Hanafi requires certainty (yaqīn) for imposing full arsh.
4
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, a Hanafi judge orders expert assessment (hukumat 'adl) to determine compensation individually; a Shafi'i judge awards full diyah (e.g., 100 camels or equivalent) regardless of actual functional loss.
Both schools agree that if health is known, full diyah is due by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي أصبع زائدة، وعين صبي، وذكره ، ولسانه، لو لم يعلم الصحة بما دل على نظره، وتحرك ذكره، وكلامه حكومة عدل (وفي أصبع زائدة، وعين صبي، وذكره ، ولسانه، لو لم يعلم الصحة بما دل على نظره، وتحرك ذكره، وكلامه حكومة عدل ) ، هذا عندنا)، وعند الشافعي يجب ديَّة كاملة ; لأنَّ الغالب الصحة ، أما إن عَلِمَ صحة هذه الأعضاء، فالواجب الدية الكاملة اتفاقاً. [١] قوله : حكومة عدل ; تشريفاً للآدمي، وهذا عندنا ; لأن المقصود من هذه الأعضاء المنفعة، فإذا لم تعلم صحتها لا يجب الأرش الكامل بالشك، والظاهر لا يصح حجّة للإلزام ، بخلاف المارن والأذن الشاخصة ; لأن المقصود هو الجمال، وقد فوته على الكمال.
And for a supernumerary finger, and a child's eye, and his penis, and his tongue: if health is not known by what indicates his vision, movement of his penis, and his speech, then just assessment (hukumat 'adl). This is according to us. And according to al-Shafi'i, a full diyah is obligatory because the norm is health. But if the health of these organs is known, then full diyah is obligatory by consensus. [Footnote 1] He said: 'Just assessment' is an honor for the human. This is according to us because the purpose of these organs is benefit; if their health is not known, the full arsh is not obligatory due to doubt, and the outward appearance is not a valid proof for imposition, unlike the nose and protruding ear because their purpose is beauty, and he has destroyed it completely.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This is a ruling on injuries to organs whose function is not verifiable. The Hanafi position is that if we cannot ascertain whether the organ is healthy and functional, the compensation is not a fixed amount but determined by a just assessment (hukumat 'adl). Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the default assumption is health, so full diyah is due. The Hanafi response is that function is the purpose, and without proof of function, the full arsh is not obligatory because it would be based on doubt. The commentary clarifies that the outward appearance (zahir) is not sufficient evidence to impose liability, unlike prominent body parts like the nose and ear where cosmetic value is the purpose.

Key Terms
حكومة عدل (hukūmat 'adl): A just assessment of compensation by a qualified expert, not a fixed amount.
ديّة (diyah): Full blood money for a life or a body part.
أرش (arsh): Fixed compensation for injury to a body part.
مارن (mārin): The tip or fleshy part of the nose (considered cosmetic).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Hukumat 'adl
C) Half diyah
D) No compensation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires hukumat 'adl (just assessment) for injuries to organs when health is unproven, while the Shafi'i school awards full diyah based on default presumption of health.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Absorption of Mudihah Arsh into Diyah for Loss of Intellect or Hair
Ruling on whether mudihah arsh is absorbed into diyah for loss of intellect or hair

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on absorption of mudihah arsh into diyah for loss of intellect or hair and explain the disagreement with Zufar.

Core Idea
When a mudihah causes loss of intellect or hair, its arsh is absorbed into the diyah; not so for sensory losses.

This lesson examines a specific rule in diyat (blood money) concerning head wounds. When a mudihah (a wound that exposes the bone) causes additional harm like loss of intellect or permanent hair loss, the question arises whether the fixed compensation (arsh) for the wound is added to the diyah for the lost function or absorbed into it. The Hanafi school has a nuanced answer, with a notable internal disagreement from Zufar.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the arsh of a mudihah enters into the diyah when the wound causes loss of intellect or loss of head hair, but not when it causes loss of hearing, sight, or speech.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (a Hanafi dissenter) holds that the arsh never enters the diyah; both compensations are always separate.
Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Zufar argues that each injury is independent, so the arsh for the mudihah and the diyah for the loss are separate, like injuries to different organs.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis distinguish: intellect and hair are directly related to the head (the locus of the wound), so loss of intellect nullifies all bodily benefit (like death), and loss of hair is a single cause. Sensory losses (sight, hearing, speech) are not tied to the head in the same way, so they remain separate.
The Hanafi response uses qiyas (analogy) to death and hand paralysis.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, Hanafi judges combine compensations for mudihah and loss of intellect/hair, paying only one diyah. Zufar's view would require separate payments for each harm.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل أرسُ مُوضحة أذهبت عقلَهُ أو شعر رأسه في الدية (٣) ، وإن ذهب سمعه ، أو بصره ، أو نطقه لا (۳) ، هذا عندنا ، وعند زفره : لا يدخل في ذهاب في الكل. (1) لأن المقصود من هذه الأعضاء المنفعة، فإذا لم تعلم صحتها لم يجب الأرش كاملاً; لأنه لا يجب بالشك، والظاهر لا يصلح حجّة للإلزام بخلاف المارن والأذن الشاخصة من الصبي ; لأن جميع المقصود منها الجمال، وقد فوّته على الكمال. ينظر : فتح باب العناية» (٣ : ٣٦٢). (۲) ينظر: «النكت»(۳: ۳۸۱)، وغيرها. (۳) يعني إذا شُجّ رجلٌ موضحة فذهب عقله أو شعر رأسه ولم ينبت دخل أرسُ الموضحة في الدية ; لأن فوات العقل يبطل منفعة . الأعضاء إذ لا ينتفع بدونه ، فصار كما إذا أوضحه فمات، وأرسُ الموضحة يجب بفواتِ جزء من الشعر حتى لو نبت الشعر سقط أرشها، والدية وجبت بفوات الشعر، وقد تعلقا جميعاً بسبب واحد وهو فواتُ الشعر، فيدخل الجزء في الكلِّ كمن قطع أصبع رجل فشلت به يده ينظر: «درر الحكام»(٢ : ١٠٦).
And the arsh of a mudihah that takes away his intellect or the hair of his head enters into the diyah (for the intellect or hair). And if his hearing, sight, or speech goes, it does not enter. This is according to us. And according to Zufar: it does not enter in all cases. [Footnote 2] He said: 'In the diyah' because the loss of intellect nullifies the benefit of all organs, so the part enters. [Footnote 3] He said: 'No' i.e., it does not enter, rather he is liable for the arsh of the mudihah along with the diyah because each one is a separate injury below the threshold of life, and the benefit is specific to it, so it resembles separate organs. When injury occurs to separate organs, the arsh of one does not enter into the other. (1) Because the purpose of these organs is benefit; if their health is not known, the full arsh is not obligatory because it is not obligatory by doubt, and the outward appearance is not a valid proof for imposition, unlike the nose and protruding ear of a child because all the purpose is beauty, and he has destroyed it completely. Refer to Fath Bab al-'Inayah (3:362). (2) Refer to al-Nukat (3:381) and others. (3) Meaning: if a man is struck with a mudihah and his intellect or head hair goes and does not regrow, the arsh of the mudihah enters into the diyah because the loss of intellect nullifies the benefit of organs since there is no benefit without it, so it becomes as if he struck him with a mudihah and he died. The arsh of the mudihah is obligatory due to the loss of part of hair, such that if the hair regrows, its arsh falls. The diyah is obligatory due to the loss of hair, and both are connected by a single cause which is the loss of hair, so the part enters into the whole, like a man whose finger is cut and his hand becomes paralyzed. Refer to Durar al-Hukkam (2:106).
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the fixed compensation (arsh) for a head wound that exposes bone (mudihah) is absorbed into the full diyah when the same injury also causes loss of intellect or total loss of head hair. The Hanafi ruling is that when the mudihah causes loss of intellect or permanent loss of hair, the arsh of the mudihah is included within the diyah for the intellect or hair—only one diyah is paid. However, if the mudihah causes loss of hearing, sight, or speech, the arsh of the mudihah is separate and must be paid in addition to the diyah for the lost sense. This is because the head is the locus of intellect and hair, so injuries to the head share a single cause; however, the head is not the locus for sensory organs like eyes and ears, so those injuries are independent. Zufar disagreed and held that the mudihah arsh never enters the diyah; it is always separate. The commentary provides analogies: loss of intellect is like death (all benefit gone), and loss of hair is like loss of a limb's function. If the hair regrows, the arsh for the mudihah is dropped, confirming that the two compensations are linked.

Key Terms
موضحة (mūḍiḥah): A head wound that exposes the bone.
أرش (arsh): Fixed compensation for a wound like mudihah.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid for loss of life or a bodily function.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When it causes loss of sight
B) When it causes loss of hearing
C) When it causes loss of intellect or hair
D) When it does not cause any additional loss

Key Takeaway
The arsh of a mudihah is absorbed into diyah when the same injury causes loss of intellect or permanent hair loss; for sensory losses, compensation is separate.

No Qisas When Both Eyes Lost: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on qisas when mudihah causes loss of both eyes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over qisas for mudihah with eye loss and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
If a mudihah causes loss of both eyes, no qisas; full diyah is paid for both injuries.

This lesson examines a nuanced case where a head wound (mudihah) leads to the loss of both eyes. The question is whether the attacker faces qisas (retaliation) for the wound and the eyes. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions differ: Abu Hanifa rules out qisas entirely, considering the eye loss as a greater harm that subsumes the wound; the Sahibayn allow qisas for the wound but not for the eyes. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principle that qisas requires precise equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the harm
The act is a mudihah (head wound exposing bone) that results in the loss of both eyes. Determine whether qisas is applicable to each harm.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that there is no qisas at all; rather, full diyah is wajib for both the mudihah and the eyes.
His view is presented first in the text.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that qisas applies to the mudihah, and diyah for the loss of eyes.
They distinguish between the wound and the eye loss as separate injuries.
4
Analyse the reasoning
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the head is the locus of both injuries; the loss of eyes is a greater harm that makes exact retaliation impossible. The Sahibayn reason: the mudihah is a distinct wound capable of qisas, while eye loss is not subject to qisas.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under Abu Hanifa only financial compensation is due, while under the Sahibayn the attacker may face both qisas and diyah.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: no qisas, full diyah for both. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This is the position from al-Jami' (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله : وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ; ظاهره أن مذهب أبي حنيفة له عدم القود عند ذهاب العينين، ووجوب الدية فيه، وفي الشيخ ،موضحه، وإن مذهب الصاحبين القصاص في الموضحة، والدية في العينين. والمفهوم من «الهداية» أن مذهب أبي حنيفة له عدم القـود فقط ، وقول الصاحبين لهذه القصاص في الموضحة ، أما وجوب الدية فيهما فلازم على مذهب الإمام ، وقول المشايخ ، وكذلك وجوب الدية في العينين لازم على قولهما ، وقول المشايخ . وعبارة «الهداية» هكذا: «وفي «الجامع»: ومن شجّ رجلاً موضحة، فذهبت عيناه فلا قصاص في ذلك عند أبي حنيفة له ، قالوا : وينبغي أن تجب الدية فيهما ، وقالا : في الموضحة القصاص، قالوا : وينبغي ان تجب الدية في العينين». انتهى
And no retaliation (qisas) if his both eyes are lost, rather diyah is due in both (i.e., in the mudihah and the two eyes, full diyah). This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: in the mudihah, retaliation; and in the two eyes, diyah. [Footnote 1] He said: 'And this is according to Abu Hanifa' apparently meaning that Abu Hanifa's position is no retaliation when both eyes are lost, and diyah is obligatory in it (the mudihah) and in the two eyes, and that the two companions' position is retaliation in the mudihah and diyah in the eyes. And what is understood from al-Hidayah is that Abu Hanifa's position is only no retaliation, and the two companions' statement is retaliation in the mudihah; but the obligation of diyah in both is necessary according to the Imam's position and the statement of the mashayikh, and similarly obligation of diyah in the eyes is necessary according to their position and the statement of the mashayikh. The wording of al-Hidayah is: 'In al-Jami': And whoever strikes a man with a mudihah, and his both eyes are lost, there is no retaliation in that according to Abu Hanifa. They said: It is appropriate that diyah be obligatory in both. And the two said: in the mudihah retaliation. They said: It is appropriate that diyah be obligatory in the eyes.' End.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific case where a person is struck on the head causing a mudihah (wound exposing bone), and as a result, the victim loses both eyes. The issue is whether qisas (retaliation) applies. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, there is no qisas in this case at all—neither for the mudihah nor for the eyes—but full diyah is payable for both. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), there is qisas for the mudihah (since it is a wound that can be exacted) but diyah for the eyes (since loss of eyes is not subject to qisas). The commentary clarifies that Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the loss of eyes is a greater harm that subsumes the mudihah, making exact retaliation impossible. The Hidayah quote shows that the obligation of diyah in both is a necessary consequence: for Abu Hanifa, diyah in both; for the Sahibayn, diyah in eyes plus qisas or diyah in mudihah (if qisas not possible). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view of no qisas and full diyah.

Key Terms
قود (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind for bodily injuries.
ديَة (diyah): Full blood money for a life or loss of a paired organ.

C) Qisas for both
D) Only diyah for the eyes, no compensation for mudihah

Key Takeaway
When a mudihah leads to loss of both eyes, the Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa: no qisas, full diyah for both injuries.

No Qisas for a Cut Finger Paralyzing the Neighbor – Abu Hanifa's View
Ruling on qisas for a cut finger causing paralysis in neighbor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on cutting a finger that paralyzes an adjacent finger, explain the concept of sariyah (spreading injury), and apply the principle that qisas requires exact equivalence (mumathala).

Core Idea
When an injury spreads to another part, qisas is impossible — exact equivalence is lost, so diyah becomes wajib.

In Hanafi fiqh, retaliation (qisas) for bodily injuries requires exact equivalence. But what if a single act of cutting causes harm beyond the intended site? This lesson examines a case where cutting one finger paralyzes its neighbor. Abu Hanifa rules that qisas is not applicable because the injury is 'sariyah' (spreading) and exact retaliation is impossible. Instead, diyah (blood money) for both fingers becomes wajib. This ruling highlights a key principle in injury compensation: the impossibility of retribution when harm is indirect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
The case: a person cuts another's finger, and as a result, the adjacent finger becomes paralyzed (though not cut). Ruling: no qisas for either finger; instead, full diyah for both fingers is wajib.
This is the Hanafi position as per Abu Hanifa.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text explicitly says 'this is according to Abu Hanifa', implying that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) may differ. Their likely view: qisas applies to the cut finger itself, since the cut was direct, while the paralysis is separate.
The Sahibayn's position is not fully detailed here but is inferred from the phrase.
3
Analyze the proof: concept of sariyah
Abu Hanifa argues that the first injury is 'sariyah' (spreading). The cut causes the neighbor to become paralyzed indirectly. Since qisas requires exact equivalence (mumathala), and the paralysis cannot be exactly replicated, qisas is impossible. The proof is rational (dalil aqli): lack of ability to control the spread.
Sariyah is a key Hanafi concept in injury compensation.
4
Determine the compensation
Since qisas is not possible, diyah becomes wajib. The diyah for two fingers is one-fifth of the full diyah (each finger is one-tenth).
The full diyah is 100 camels (or equivalent). So two fingers = 20 camels.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
This ruling extends to any injury that causes indirect harm to another body part. Whenever exact retaliation is impossible due to the nature of the injury, the compensation shifts to diyah.
This is a general rule: 'Every injury that is sariyah precludes qisas.'

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage addresses a case where a person cuts another's finger, and as a result, the adjacent finger also becomes paralyzed (though not cut). The question is whether qisas applies to the cut finger or the paralyzed finger. Abu Hanifa rules that there is no qisas for either finger because the injury is 'sariyah' (spreading)—the cut caused the paralysis indirectly. Ruling on Diyah for Finger Joint Cut and Black Tooth Break
Passage stating rulings on diyah and hukuma for finger and tooth injuries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on compensation for a finger cut at the top joint with paralysis, and for a broken black tooth, and apply the concept of hukuma.

Core Idea
Paralysis after a cut requires hukuma; a broken black tooth requires full diyah.

This lesson examines two cases from the diyat chapter: first, a finger injury that causes paralysis in the remaining part, and second, a broken black tooth. The Hanafi school specifies when a fixed diyah applies and when a discretionary hukuma is assessed. The text also addresses a scenario of qisas on a tooth that grows back, clarifying that retaliation must ensure exact equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage discusses two distinct injuries: a finger cut at the top joint with paralysis of the remaining part, and a black tooth broken in half.
2
Compensation for the finger case
For the finger, the joint has a fixed diyah. The paralyzed part has no fixed amount, so hukuma (discretionary compensation assessed by a judge) is required.
Hukuma is based on the reduction in the victim's value, not on a fixed schedule.
3
Compensation for the tooth case
For a black tooth, if half is broken, the full diyah of the tooth is due, not half.
The tooth is considered a single functional unit; loss of half destroys the benefit of the whole.
4
The qisas scenario
If qisas is carried out on a tooth and it later grows back, the retaliator must pay diyah because exact equivalence cannot be ensured.
The retaliation was not proper because one must wait a year to see if the tooth regenerates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي أصبع قطع مفصله الأعلى، وشلّ ما بَقي، بل دية المفصل، والحكومة فيما بقي، ولا بكسر نصف سن أسود باقيها، بل كل دِيَةِ السِّنِّ. ويُحِبُّ الأَرسُ على مَن أقاد سنه ثم نبتت أو قلعها فردت إلى الجراحة التي تعمل قصاصاً، قد تكون سارية، وقد لا تكون سارية، إذ ليس في وسعه فعل ذلك، فلا يكون مثلاً للأولى، ولا قصاص بدون المماثلة فيجب المال.
And concerning a finger from which the top joint is cut, and the remainder becomes paralyzed, then the diyah of the joint and the hukuma for what remains. And no breaking of half of a black tooth while the other half remains, but rather the full diyah of the tooth. And the blood money is obligatory upon one who retaliated (qisas) on his tooth and then it grew back, or he pulled it out and it returned to the wound that acts as qisas; it may be continuous or may not be continuous, since it is not in his power to do that, so it is not like the first, and there is no qisas without equivalence, so money is obligatory.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rulings: first, for a finger where the top joint is cleanly cut and the rest of the finger becomes paralyzed due to the injury, the offender must pay the diyah for the joint (which is a specified amount) plus a discretionary amount (hukuma) for the paralyzed portion because no fixed diyah exists for that part. Second, for a black tooth: if half of a black tooth is broken, the entire diyah of the tooth is due, not half, because the function is lost. The text then addresses a scenario where a person has already undergone qisas on a tooth (meaning his tooth was cut off as retaliation), but the tooth grows back; in that case, the original qisas was done improperly because one must wait a year to see if the tooth grows back. Since the act was unjust, the retaliator must pay diyah (blood money) for the tooth. The reasoning is that the act of pulling or cutting may not be exactly equivalent, so money replaces qisas.

Key Terms
حكومة (hukuma): An assessed amount of compensation for an injury that has no fixed diyah; determined by a judge.
أرش (arsh): Compensation or blood money for an injury; synonymous with diyah in some contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Diyah of the joint plus hukuma for the paralyzed part

D) Only hukuma for the whole finger

Key Takeaway

Finger Diya and Black Tooth: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Qisas
Rulings on finger and tooth diyat with intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifah's ruling on diya for a paralyzed finger and half a black tooth, and explain the Sahibayn's counterposition on qisas.

Core Idea
A single act triggering partial monetary compensation cancels qisas entirely according to Abu Hanifah.

This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses two specific injury cases: a finger with a cut joint and paralyzed remainder, and a black tooth broken in half. The matn rules that full diya is due for the joint and full diya for the tooth, and no qisas. The commentary reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute: while Abu Hanifah stands by this principle, his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that qisas remains obligatory alongside diya. Understanding this ikhtilaf illuminates the school's methodology in balancing qisas and monetary compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two injury scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) a finger whose upper joint is cut and the remaining part becomes paralyzed; (2) half of a black tooth is broken while the other half remains.
Both involve partial injury to a body part.
2
State the ruling for each scenario per the matn
For the finger: diya for the cut joint and hukuma (discretionary compensation) for the paralyzed remainder. For the black tooth: full diya of a tooth, not half, because the intact half does not reduce liability.
The matn says 'بل كل دِيَةِ السِّنِّ' (rather full diya of the tooth).
3
Recognize Abu Hanifah's principle
According to Abu Hanifah, when a single injurious act partly incurs monetary compensation (diya or hukuma), qisas is dropped entirely, whether the injury affects two limbs or one limb.
This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.
4
Contrast with the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that qisas is obligatory along with diya or arsh for breaking half the tooth.
They do not accept the principle that partial monetary compensation extinguishes qisas.
5
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifah, the offender pays full diya for half a tooth but faces no retaliation. Under the Sahibayn, the victim can demand qisas on the offender's tooth in addition to compensation.
This difference affects the offender's potential physical liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي أصبع قطع مفصله الأعلى، وشلّ ما بَقي ، بل دية المفصل، والحكومة فيما بقي ، ولا بكسر نصف سن أسود باقيها ، بل كل دِيَةِ السِّنِّ. [2] قوله : ولا بكسر ... الخ ؛ أي لا قود بكسر ... الخ ؛ قال في «التكملة»: وهذا كلّه قول أبي حنيفة ، وقالا : يجب القصاص والدية أو الأرش، والأصل عنده أن الفعل الواحد إذا أوجب مالاً في البعض سقط القصاص، سواءً كانا عضوين أو عضواً واحداً لا يجب لهما.
And concerning a finger: if its upper joint is cut, and the remainder becomes paralyzed, then the diya of the joint, and the hukuma (assessment) for the remainder. And concerning breaking half of a black tooth with its remainder: rather, the full diya of the tooth. [2] His saying: 'And not by breaking half...' meaning no qisas for breaking half...; he said in 'al-Takmilah': This is all the saying of Abu Hanifah, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: qisas and diya or arsh are obligatory. And the principle according to him (Abu Hanifah) is that when a single act necessitates monetary compensation in part, qisas is dropped, whether it concerns two limbs or one limb; it is not obligatory for them.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn on injuries to a finger and a black tooth. For a finger, if the top phalanx is cut off and the rest of the finger becomes paralyzed (shalla), the liability is a fixed diya for the cut joint, and for the paralyzed remainder, a hukuma (discretionary compensation assessed by a judge) is due. For breaking half of a black tooth while the other half remains, the perpetrator must pay the full diya of a tooth, not half, because the intact half does not reduce the liability. The commentary by al-Takmilah notes that this is Abu Hanifah's position. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that qisas is obligatory for the half of the tooth, along with diya or arsh. Abu Hanifah's underlying principle (asl) is that if a single injurious act partly incurs monetary compensation, qisas is waived entirely—whether the act affects two distinct limbs or a single limb. This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.

Key Terms
دِيَة (diyah): Fixed monetary compensation for injury to a body part or homicide.
حُكُومَة (hukūmah): Discretionary compensation assessed by a judge for an injury that has no fixed diya.
شَلّ (shall): Paralysis or loss of function; here, the remaining part of a finger becomes paralyzed after the joint is cut.
قِصَاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment akin to the injury inflicted (eye for an eye).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, what is the ruling for breaking half of a black tooth?
A) Half diya of a tooth
B) Full diya of a tooth and no qisas
C) Full diya of a tooth and qisas
D) Only qisas, no diya

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, partial injury to a limb may trigger full diya for the part, and the question of qisas depends on whether the same act partly incurs monetary compensation—Abu Hanifah's principle excludes qisas in such cases.

Arsh for a Regrown Tooth: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Unity of Act
Intra-Hanafi dispute on arsh for a regrown tooth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifah and Sahibayn/Zufar on arsh for a regrown tooth after qisas, and explain how the concepts of shubha and sirāyah drive the dispute.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifah: act is one, shubha drops qisas → arsh. Sahibayn: two acts → qisas then arsh.

When a person takes qisas (retaliation) for a tooth by having the offender's tooth removed, and that tooth later grows back (or is reinserted), a subtle but critical dispute arises among the Hanafi imams. Does the retaliator owe anything more? The matn states that arsh (compensation) is wajib. But why not qisas for a second injury? This lesson unpacks the competing analogies and the role of shubha (doubt) in the Hanafi law of qisas.

Guided Path
1
State the case and the initial ruling
The case: a person takes qisas for his tooth (offender's tooth removed). Then the offender's tooth grows back or is reinserted. The matn says arsh is obligatory upon the person who took the retaliation.
This is the starting point; the commentary then explains the internal ikhtilaf.
2
Identify the position of Sahibayn and Zufar
They hold: no qisas for the first act (the retaliation itself is lawful), but for the second event (the regrowth/reinsertion) arsh is due. For them, the act is in two distinct loci, so they are two independent injuries.
They analogize to a bullet that passes through one person killing a second: qisas for the first (intentional) and diya for the second (mistake).
3
Follow their proof (qiyas)
Their dalil: the retaliation and the regrowth are separate acts in separate places (tooth socket and regrown tooth). Shubha in one (perhaps the regrowth is not a fresh injury) does not extend to the other. The bullet analogy shows that two distinct injuries are treated separately.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the multiplicity of loci.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifah's rebuttal
Abu Hanifah argues that the act is one in reality: the single motion of removing or cutting. The place is also continuous because the tooth and its socket are connected. The end result (regrowth) creates a shubha of mistake (did the retaliation cause the regrowth?), which drops qisas entirely. The bullet analogy fails because sirāyah (penetration) between two separate souls is impossible.
Key: sirāyah only works within one person; here the act and its consequence stay within one continuous entity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifah, the retaliator only pays arsh (monetary compensation) for the tooth. Under Sahibayn/Zufar, he would also be subject to qisas (retaliation) for the first act (removing the tooth), and arsh for the second event (regrowth).
The mufta bihi is often Abu Hanifah's view, as it is more cautious in applying qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُحِبُّ الأَرسُ على مَن أقاد سنه ثم نبتت أو قلعها فردت إلى [1] قوله : وعندهما وعند زفر لا يقتص من الأول وفي الثاني أرشها ؛ لأن الفعل في محلين، فيكون جنايتين مبتدأتين، فالشبهة في إحداهما لا تتعدى إلى بالأخرى ، كمَن رمى إلى رجل عمداً فأصابته ونفذ منه إلى آخر فقتله، يجب القود في الأول. الجواب عن جانب الإمام: إنّ الفعل فيما نحن فيه واحد حقيقة، وهو الحركة القائمة، وكذا المحل متحد من وجه ؛ لاتصال أحدهما بالآخر، فأورثت نهايته شبهة الخطأ، وفي البداية بخلاف النفسين، فإنّ السِّرايةَ إنّما تكون تبعاً، وهو إنما يتحقق في شخص واحد، فالفعلُ في النفس الثانية مباشرة على حدة، ليس بسراية الجناية الأولى، إذ لا يتصوّر السراية من نفس إلى نفس، فلا بد أن يجعل ذلك في حكم فعل على حدة ، وهو خطأ. كذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية».
And arsh is obligatory upon one who takes retaliation for his tooth (i.e., has the offender's tooth removed in qisas) and then it (the offender's tooth) grows back, or he pulls it out and it is returned... [1] His saying: 'And with them (Sahibayn) and with Zufar: he does not exact qisas from the first, and for the second its arsh.' Because the act is in two places, so they become two independent injuries. The doubt (shubha) in one does not extend to the other, like one who intentionally shoots a person, and it hits him and penetrates to another and kills him: qisas is obligatory for the first. The answer from the Imam's side: The act in our case is one in reality, namely the originating motion, and likewise the place is unified from one aspect due to the connection of one to the other. So its end gave rise to a doubt of mistake, unlike the case of two souls, for the penetration (sirāyah) is only subsidiary, and it is only realized in one person. The act on the second soul is a direct act separately, not a penetration of the first injury, since penetration from one soul to another is inconceivable. So it must be considered as a separate act, and it is an error. Thus in some marginalia of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where a person takes retaliation (qisas) for his tooth, meaning the offender's tooth is removed. Then, the offender's tooth grows back (or the tooth is pulled out and reinserted). The question is: does the original retaliator owe anything? The matn states that arsh (compensation) is obligatory upon the person who took the retaliation. The commentary then reports the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) and Zufar: they say that no qisas is taken from the first act (the retaliation itself), and for the second event (the regrowth or reinsertion), arsh is due. Their reasoning: the act (retaliation) and the regrowth are two separate incidents in two different loci, so they are two independent injuries. The shubha (doubt) in one does not affect the other. They analogize to a manslaughter scenario: if a man intentionally shoots someone, the bullet hits the first person and then passes through to kill a second, qisas is obligatory for the first (intentional), and the second is considered a mistake (so diya only). The response from Imam Abu Hanifah's side is that the act in this case is one in reality: the motion of pulling or cutting. The place is also unified because the tooth and its socket are connected. The end result (the regrowth or reinsertion) gives rise to a shubha of mistake, so qisas drops. In contrast, when two persons are killed by a single shot, the acts are separate because sirāyah (penetration) cannot transfer from one soul to another. Therefore, in the tooth case, the act is considered a single act with a shubha at its end, so only arsh is due. This is a typical Hanafi istihsan argument.

Key Terms
أَرْش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a body part that does not have a fixed diya, or in cases of doubt.
شُبْهَة (shubhah): Doubt or uncertainty that prevents the application of hadd or qisas, leading to monetary compensation.
سِرَايَة (sirāyah): The extension or penetration of an injury from one locus to another, e.g., a bullet passing through the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, why is arsh due and not qisas when a tooth regrows after retaliation?
A) Because the act is considered two separate acts
B) Because the regrowth is a new injury caused by the retaliator
C) Because the act is one in reality and its end creates a shubha
D) Because the tooth is not a body part worthy of qisas

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a tooth regrows after qisas, shubha from the continuity of the act drops qisas, and compensation (arsh) becomes wajib, though Sahibayn disagree.

Tooth Reinsertion and Wound Healing: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation for partially healed body parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on arsh for a reinserted tooth and a healed wound, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A healed wound may still entitle compensation, but Abu Hanifah says no; the Sahibayn disagree.

When a tooth is pulled out and later reinserted, or a wound heals without leaving a trace, what compensation is due? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents Abu Hanifah's view that arsh is only for a tooth that fails to fully restore function, and no arsh for a healed wound. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, argue for compensation for the pain or treatment costs. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses: (a) a reinserted tooth that may or may not fully heal, and (b) a wound that heals without a trace.
2
State Abu Hanifah's ruling for the tooth
Arsh is wajib unless the tooth returns to full function and beauty (unlikely). If it fully restores, no arsh.
See commentary [1] from al-Kifayah.
3
State Abu Hanifah's ruling for the healed wound
No arsh because the defect (shayn) is removed; pain (manfa'ah) is not compensable.
Abu Hanifah's view as defended in the text.
4
Identify the Sahibayn's objections
Abu Yusuf says arsh for pain via hukuma; Muhammad says wajib to pay doctor and medicine costs.
These views are recorded in the commentary.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
The commentary defends Abu Hanifah: benefits (manafi') are not compensable except by contract or shubhah. Thus the mufta bihi appears to be Abu Hanifah's view.
This is a talil (legal reasoning) based on a usul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكانها، ونبت عليه اللحم لا إن قلعت فنبتت أخرى، أو التحمت شجة أو جُرح بضرب ولم يبق أثره [1] قوله : يجب الأرش على من قلع... الخ؛ في «الكفاية»: قال شيخ الإسلام هذا إذا لم تعد إلى حالها الأولى بعد النبات في المنفعة والجمال، والغالب أن لا تعود إلى تلك الحالة؛ لأنَّ المقلوعَ لا يزلق بالعصب والعروق في الغالب، فيكون وجود هذا النبات والعدمُ بمنزلة واحدة، وأما لو تصوّر عود الجمال والمنفعة بالإنبات لم يكن على القالع شيء، كما لو نبت السنّ المقلوعة كما في «الذخيرة». [2] قوله : وهو حكومة العدل؛ لأنّ الشين إن زالَ فالألم الحاصل ما زال، فيجب تقويم الفائت بالألم، وهو الصحة وهو ما زال؛ لأنّ زوال الفائت بحصول البدل، ولم يحصل، لكن حصل صحة أخرى في زمان آخر، غير قائمة مقام الفائت. كذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية». [3] قوله: تجب أجرة الطبيب، وثمن الدواء؛ لأنه إنما لزمه أجرة الطبيب وثمن الدواء بفعله، فصار كأنّ الشاجّ أخذ ذلك من ماله، والجواب عن قول أبي يوسف له ومحمد له أن المنافع على أصلنا لا تتقوم إلا بعقد وبشبهة، ولم يوجد في حق الجاني.
Returned to its place, and flesh grew over it. Not if it was pulled out and another grew, or a shajjah (head wound) healed, or a wound by a blow and no trace remained. [1] His saying: 'Arsh is obligatory upon the one who pulled out...' in al-Kifayah: Shaykh al-Islam said: This is when it does not return to its original state after growth in function and beauty, and the norm is that it does not return to that state because the pulled tooth is not reestablished with nerves and veins usually. So the existence of this growth and its absence are equivalent. But if one imagines the return of beauty and function by growth, then nothing is owed upon the puller, just as when the pulled tooth grows back as in al-Dhakhirah. [2] His saying: 'And it is a hukuma of the just (arbitrator)' because if the defect (shayn) is removed, the pain that occurred remains, so the lost element (pain) must be assessed. And that is health (sihhah), which has not returned. Because the loss is not substituted by a replacement, but another health has occurred at a different time, not substituting for the lost. Thus in some marginalia of al-Hidayah. [3] His saying: 'The wages of the doctor and the price of medicine are obligatory' because he (the injurer) incurred the wages of the doctor and price of medicine by his action, so it is as if the wounder took that from his wealth. And the answer to the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is that benefits according to our principle are not quantifiable except by contract or shubhah, and this is absent regarding the injurer.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on a tooth that was pulled out and then reinserted and healed (flesh grew over it). The matn states that arsh is obligatory upon the person who pulled it out, except if the tooth was pulled out and another tooth grew in its place (then no arsh according to Abu Hanifah). Similarly, if a head wound (shajjah) heals completely, or a wound from a blow leaves no trace, the ruling is discussed. Commentary [1] from al-Kifayah explains that arsh is due only if the tooth does not return to its original function and beauty. Usually, a reimplanted tooth does not reattach to nerves and vessels, so the regrowth is functionally useless; hence arsh is due. If it did fully restore, then no arsh (like a naturally regrown tooth). Commentary [2] discusses the case where a wound heals without a trace: Abu Hanifah said no arsh because the defect (shayn) is gone. But Abu Yusuf says there is arsh for the pain (hukuma). The marginalia explains that the pain is a lost benefit (sihhah) that has not been replaced, so it merits compensation despite the defect's removal. Commentary [3] reports Muhammad's view that the injurer must pay the doctor's fee and medicine cost. Then a response to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is given: according to Hanafi usul, benefits (manafi') are not compensable except by contract or shubhah, and here the injurer has no such basis. So Abu Hanifah's view (no arsh for pain or treatment) is upheld. This is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute with talil (legal reasoning).

Key Terms
شَجَّة (shajjah): A wound on the head or face; here, a head wound that heals completely without a scar.
شَيْن (shayn): Defect or disfigurement caused by the injury.
مَنْفَعَة (manfa'ah): Benefit or utility; here, the benefit of health or pain relief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Arsh is obligatory for the pain caused
B) No arsh is due
C) The cost of treatment is wajib
D) Hukuma is due

Key Takeaway
Compensation for a healed wound is not automatic: Abu Hanifah's view (no arsh) is upheld in the commentary, though the Sahibayn offer alternative positions.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Arsh for Tooth Extraction and Wound Healing
Intra-Hanafi dispute: conditions for arsh after tooth extraction and wound healing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding arsh for a pulled tooth replaced by a new tooth, and evaluate the basis of each position.

Core Idea
When a tooth is pulled and a new one grows, Abu Hanifa says no arsh; the Sahibayn say yes—each based on how they view the injury's persistence.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya details an intra-Hanafi disagreement on when arsh (compensation) is owed after a tooth extraction or a wound that heals without a trace. The three eponymous imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—offer differing rulings based on their assessment of whether the injury effectively persists. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi principles of compensation for bodily harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two cases: (1) a tooth is pulled and a new tooth grows; (2) a wound heals without any remaining mark. Note that the first scenario excludes the case where the original tooth is reinserted and flesh grows over it (where arsh is owed by agreement).
2
State the position of each imam
For each scenario, state the ruling according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. In the tooth case: Abu Hanifa says no arsh; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say arsh is due. In the wound case: Abu Hanifa says no arsh; Abu Yusuf says ars al-alam (pain compensation) via hukumat al-adl; Muhammad says the doctor's fee and medicine cost are due.
This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf recorded in the later commentaries.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil) for each position
Abu Hanifa reasons that a new tooth is a fresh blessing from Allah; the injury is nullified. Similarly, a healed wound without trace means the defect (shayn) that necessitated arsh no longer exists. Abu Yusuf argues that the pain remains even if the mark disappears, so compensation is due for that separate loss. Muhammad treats the medical expenses as damages caused by the injurer's action.
The underlying principle: Abu Hanifa focuses on the disappearance of the visible defect; the Sahibayn consider ongoing or secondary harms.
4
Identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
Although the text does not explicitly declare a preferred view, the commentary implies that Abu Hanifa's view is relied upon, consistent with the later Hanafi tradition (e.g., al-Hidayah). The reasoning is that on the Hanafi principle, benefits (manafi') are not compensable without a contract or shubha, so the Sahibayn's position is weaker.
Authority weight: the positions are recorded as nawadir (rare transmissions) but the mufta bihi aligns with the majority of later scholars.
5
Apply to a simple case
Given a scenario: A man strikes another's face, the wound heals completely with no scar. According to the mufta bihi (Abu Hanifa), no arsh is due. According to Abu Yusuf, the victim would receive ars al-alam via equitable assessment. According to Muhammad, only the doctor's fee and medicine.
The practical difference is the amount and type of compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية مكانها، ونبت عليه اللحم لا إن قلعت فنبتت أخرى، أو التحمت شجة أو جُرح بضرب ولم يبق أثره [١]- مكانها، ونبت عليه اللحم) : أي يجب الأرسُ علـي مـن قلـع سـن غـيره فـرد صاحب السِّنْ سنَّهُ إلى مكانها ، فنبت عليها اللحم، وإنما يجب الأرش ؛ لأن نبات اللحم لا اعتبار له ؛ لأنَّ العروق لا تعود. (لا إن قلعت فنبتت أخرى، فإنَّه لا يجب الأرسُ على القالع عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لأن الجناية انعدمت معنى، كما إذا قلع سن صبي فنبتت أخرى لا يجب الأرش بالإجماع، وعندهما يجب الأرش؛ لأنَّ الجناية قد تحققت ، والحادثة نعمة مبتدأة من الله الله. (أو التحمت شجّة أو جُرحَ بضرب ولم يبقَ أثره ) ، فإِنَّهُ يسقط الأرسُ عند أبي حنيفة الله لزوال الشين الموجب، وعند أبي يوسف له : عليه أرسُ الألم، وهو حكومة العدل ، قيل : ينظرُ إن الإنسان بكم يجرح نفسه مثل هذه الجراحة ، فإنَّ بعض النَّاسِ يخرجُ نفسه ويأخذ على ذلك شيئاً، وعند محمد ه : تجب أجرةً الطبيب وثمن الدواء. [1] قوله : يجب الأرش على من قلع... الخ ؛ في «الكفاية»: قال شيخ الإسلام هذا إذا لم تعد إلى حالها الأولى بعد النبات في المنفعة والجمال، والغالب أن لا تعود إلى تلك الحالة ؛ لأنَّ المقلوعَ لا يزلق بالعصب والعروق في الغالب، فيكون وجود هذا النبات والعدمُ بمنزلة واحدة ، وأما لو تصوّر عود الجمال والمنفعة بالإنبات لم يكن على القالع شيء، كما لو نبت السنّ المقلوعة كما في «الذخيرة». [٢]قوله : وهو حكومة العدل ؛ لأنّ الشين إن زالَ فالألم الحاصل ما زال، فيجب تقويم الفائت بالألم، وهو الصحة وهو ما زال ؛ لأنّ زوال الفائت بحصول البدل، ولم يحصل، لكن حصل صحة أخرى في زمان آخر ، غير قائمة مقام الفائت. كذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية». (٣) قوله: تجب أجرة الطبيب، وثمن الدواء؛ لأنه إنما لزمه أجرة الطبيب وثمن الدواء بفعله، فصار كأنّ الشاجّ أخذ ذلك من ماله ، والجواب عن قول أبي يوسف له ومحمد له أن المنافع على أصلنا لا تتقوم إلا بعقد وبشبهة، ولم يوجد في حق الجاني،
Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah: its place, and flesh grew over it—not if it was pulled out and another grew, or a wound or injury healed with a blow and its trace did not remain. [1] 'Its place, and flesh grew over it': meaning that arsh is obligatory on the one who pulled out another's tooth, then the owner of the tooth put his tooth back in its place, and flesh grew over it. Arsh is obligatory because the growth of flesh is not considered, since the roots do not return. (Not if it was pulled out and another grew, then arsh is not obligatory on the extractor according to Abu Hanifa, because the injury is effectively nullified, just as when the tooth of a child is pulled out and another grows, arsh is not obligatory by consensus; according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], arsh is obligatory because the injury has occurred, and the new event is a fresh blessing from Allah). (Or a wound or injury healed with a blow and its trace did not remain), then arsh falls according to Abu Hanifa due to the disappearance of the defect that caused it; according to Abu Yusuf, he owes ars al-alam, which is hukumat al-adl. It is said: one considers how much a person would injure himself with such an injury, for some people would take money for it. According to Muhammad, the fee of the doctor and the price of medicine are obligatory. [1] His statement: 'Arsh is obligatory on the one who pulled out...' in al-Kifayah: Shaikh al-Islam said this applies if it does not return to its original state after growth in benefit and beauty, and generally it does not return to that state, because the pulled-out tooth does not adhere with nerves and blood vessels generally, so the existence of this growth and its absence are the same. But if the return of beauty and benefit through growth could be imagined, then nothing is owed, just like when the pulled-out tooth grows back as in al-Dhakhira. [2] His statement: 'It is hukumat al-adl; because if the defect has disappeared, the pain that occurred has not disappeared, so the lost [benefit] must be evaluated by the pain, which is health that has disappeared; because the loss of the lost is compensated by obtaining a substitute, and no substitute has occurred, but another health occurred at another time, not standing in place of the lost. Thus in some glosses of al-Hidayah. (3) His statement: 'The fee of the doctor and price of medicine are obligatory; because he obliged them by his action, so it is as if the injurer took that from his property. The answer to the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is that benefits according to our principle are not compensable except by contract or by doubt, and that is not present with respect to the injurer.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning liability for arsh (compensation) when a tooth is pulled out and replaced, growing flesh, or when a wound heals without trace. The text first states the ruling: if the tooth is returned to its place and flesh grows, arsh is still obligatory because the roots do not reattach; the growth is not considered. However, if the tooth is pulled out and a new tooth grows, according to Abu Hanifa no arsh is due because the injury is nullified, analogous to a child's tooth where consensus holds no arsh. But Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that arsh is obligatory because the injury occurred and the new tooth is a fresh blessing. Similarly, for a wound that heals without any trace, Abu Hanifa says arsh falls because the disfigurement (shayn) that necessitated compensation is gone. Abu Yusuf says he owes ars al-alam (pain compensation) determined by hukumat al-adl (equitable assessment), considering what a person would typically accept for such an injury. Muhammad says the liable party must pay the doctor's fee and medicine cost, because he caused those expenses. The commentary then elaborates: the ruling depends on whether the original state of beauty and function returns—if it does, no liability; but this rarely happens. Abu Yusuf's view is justified because pain remains even if the mark disappears; Muhammad's view treats the expenses as damages. The final note responds that on the Hanafi principle, benefits (manafi') are not compensable except by contract or shubha, so the Sahibayn's view is weak. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but later works favor Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
الأَرْش (al-arsh): Compensation paid for a bodily injury that reduces value or function, measured by the difference in value of the body part before and after injury.
حُكُومَةُ الْعَدْل (hukumat al-adl): An equitable valuation of the loss, estimating the decrease in the victim's value as a slave (if a slave) or by analogy to free person's worth.
أَرْشُ الْأَلَم (ars al-alam): Compensation specifically for the pain caused by the injury, distinct from compensation for loss of limb or function.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, if a person's tooth is knocked out and a new one grows, does the extractor owe arsh?
A) Yes, because the injury occurred.
B) No, because the injury is nullified by the growth of a new tooth.
C) Only the doctor's fee is owed.
D) Half of the diyah is owed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative ruling is that arsh is not due for a tooth that grows back or a wound that heals without trace, because the injury is considered to have been nullified—a view derived from the principle that benefits are not independently compensable.

Qisas for Wounds Requires Healing Before Execution
Ruling with proof: qisas delayed for wounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for wounds, cite the hadith evidence, and explain why healing is required.

Core Idea
Qisas for a wound is postponed until healing because the wound might become fatal.

The Hanafi school holds that retaliation for a wound cannot be carried out immediately. The wound must first heal to confirm it was non‑lethal. This lesson examines the proof and why the Shafi'i school disagrees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is qisas for a wound. The ruling: it cannot be executed until healing.
This is a procedural restriction on executing qisas.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites a hadith: 'He forbade retaliation for a wound until its owner heals' (Ahmad, Daraqutni). Also a rational reason: wounds may spread.
Hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut, but dalala qati' due to clear prohibition.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition (nahy) indicates that qisas is not permissible until the condition 'healing' is met.
The word 'until' (حتى) implies delay.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i allows immediate qisas, like qisas for murder. The Hanafi position is based on caution.
The dispute is on whether the condition is necessary.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi (operative), supported by hadith and the risk of fatal escalation.
This is the relied-upon position.

Source Text

[١] (1) (ولا يقاد جرح إلا بعد برء) ، هذا عندنا ، وعند الشَّافِعِي ) : في الحال كما في القصاص في النفس. يقتص وعمد الصبي والمجنون خطاً، وعلى عاقلته الدية، ولا كفارة فيه، ولا حرمان إرث . فلا يغرم شيئاً . كذا في «الهداية)) (۲) [١]قوله : إلا بعد برء ؛ لما روي أنه عليه : نهى أن يقتص من جرح حتى يبرأ صاحبه (۳)، رواه أحمد والدارقطني ؛ ولأنّ الجراحات يعتبر فيها مالها ؛ لاحتمال أن تسري إلى النفس فيظهر أنه قتل ، فلا يعلم أنه جرح إلا بالبرء، فيستقر به. هكذا في «رد المحتار) () نقلا عن الزيلعي (٤) ~(0) وله : خطأ لما روي عن علي الله أنه جعل عقل المجنون على عاقلته، وقال: عمده وخطؤه سواء ؛ ولأنّ الصبي مظنة الرحمة، والعاقل الخاطئ لما استحق التخفيف، والصبي وهو أعذر أولى بهذا التخفيف والكفارة تكون بالإثم، ولا إثم لهما ؛ لأنهما مرفوعا القلم، وحرما الميراث عقوبة، وهما ليس من أهل العقوبة. كذا في «الهداية»
And no wound is retaliated except after healing. And the intentional act of a child and an insane person is [treated as] accidental, and upon their 'aqila is the diyah, and there is no kaffarah in it, and no deprivation of inheritance. [1] (1) ('And no wound is retaliated except after healing'): This is according to us [Hanafis], and according to al-Shafi'i: immediately, as in retaliation for life.) He exacts qisas. And the intentional act of a child and an insane person is accidental, and upon their 'aqila is the diyah, and there is no kaffarah in it, and no deprivation of inheritance. So he does not pay anything. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'except after healing'; because it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) forbade retaliation for a wound until its owner heals, narrated by Ahmad and al-Daraqutni; and because wounds are considered according to what they have [in terms of severity], due to the possibility that they may spread to the soul and it becomes clear that it is murder, and it is not known that it is a wound until healing, so it is established. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar quoting from al-Zayla'i. And it [the act of child/insane] is accidental because it is narrated from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he made the 'aql of the insane person upon his 'aqila, and said: his intentional and his accidental are the same; and because the child is deserving of mercy, and the erring sane person deserved leniency, and the child who is more excusable is more deserving of this leniency. And kaffarah is due for sin, and there is no sin for them because the pen is lifted from them, and they are deprived of inheritance as a punishment, and they are not worthy of punishment. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings with their proofs. First, it states that qisas (retaliation) for a wound cannot be executed until the wound has healed. This is the Hanafi position; the Shafi'i school allows immediate qisas as in retaliation for life. The proof is a hadith from Ahmad and al-Daraqutni prohibiting retaliation until healing, and a rational consideration: wounds may become fatal, so only after healing is it certain that it was a non-lethal wound. Second, it rules that the deliberate act of a minor or an insane person is treated as accidental (khatā') in terms of liability. Consequently, diyah is payable by the 'āqila (the tribal or insuring group) of the perpetrator, not by the perpetrator personally. No kaffārah (expiation) is required because kaffārah is linked to sin, and these individuals lack legal responsibility (they are not accountable). Additionally, there is no deprivation of inheritance (a punishment for intentional killing). The proof includes a precedent from 'Alī (ra) regarding the insane, and analogy: since a sane person who errs gets leniency, a child who is more excusable deserves even more. The relied-upon position is as stated in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
القِصَاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind for bodily injury, subject to conditions like healing for wounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To give the victim time to forgive.
B) Because the wound might become fatal, so until healing it is uncertain.
C) Because the perpetrator needs to apologize.
D) Because qisas is always delayed for all injuries.

Key Takeaway
Qisas for a wound is delayed until healing to ensure certainty that the injury was non-lethal, supported by hadith and rational reasoning.

Act of Child or Insane Person is Treated as Accidental
Ruling with proof: child/insane acts as accidental with consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the intentional act of a child or insane person is classified as accidental, and state the consequences on diyah, kaffarah, and inheritance.

Core Idea
The intentional act of a child or insane person is legally accidental because they lack sinful intent.

Islamic law treats the deliberate harmful acts of a child or an insane person as accidental (khatā') for liability purposes. This means the perpetrator does not personally pay; the 'āqila is responsible for diyah, and no kaffarah or inheritance deprivation applies. This lesson traces the proofs from the precedent of 'Alī and qiyas with the erring sane person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The subject: a child or insane person who intentionally causes injury or death. The ruling: their act is treated as accidental.
This changes liability from personal to communal.
2
Locate the evidences
The text provides two proofs: (1) a precedent from 'Alī (ra) for the insane; (2) qiyas (analogy) between the child and a sane person who errs.
The qiyas is based on shared illa of deserving leniency.
3
Understand the consequences
Because the act is accidental: diyah is on the 'āqila (not the perpetrator), no kaffarah (linked to sin), and no deprivation of inheritance (a punishment).
The child/insane are not legally culpable (raf' al-qalam).
4
Analyze the qiyas structure
Asl: a sane person who accidentally kills – gets leniency (diyah on 'āqila, no qisas). Illa: lack of full intent/excusability. Far': child or insane person who intentionally acts – they are even more excusable.
Thus the hukm of the asl is applied to the far'.
5
Note the absence of kaffarah and inheritance deprivation
Kaffarah is due for sin (ithm); the pen is lifted from these individuals, so no sin, no kaffarah. Deprivation of inheritance is a punishment; they are not subject to punishment.
These are derived from the principle of non-accountability.

Source Text

العَاقِلَة (al-'āqila): The group of male agnatic relatives (or tribe) liable for paying diyah on behalf of a member who caused accidental death or injury.
الكَفَّارَة (al-kaffārah): Expiation required for certain sins, such as accidental killing (freeing a slave, fasting, etc.). Here, not required for child/insane because no sin.

Qisas for Wounds and Acts of Minors and the Insane
States rulings on qisas for wounds and treatment of minors/madmen as accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for wounds, explain why it is deferred until healing, and describe how the intentional acts of minors and the insane are legally classified as accidental with liability on the aqilah.

Core Idea
Qisas for a wound waits for healing; a child's or madman's deliberate act is accidental, placing diyah on the aqilah.

In Hanafi criminal law, retaliation (qisas) for wounds is not immediate; it is deferred until the wound heals to ensure the injury is not fatal. Additionally, the deliberate acts of children and the insane are legally classified as accidental (khat'a), shifting liability from the offender to the aqilah (tribal surety). These rulings are supported by a hadith prohibiting immediate qisas, the practice of Ali ibn Abi Talib, and reasoning by analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and highlight two distinct rulings: (1) qisas for a wound only after healing, (2) the deliberate act of a child or insane person is treated as accidental.
These rulings are presented consecutively in the matn.
2
Examine the proof for the first ruling
The passage cites a hadith: the Prophet forbade qisas for a wound until its owner heals. Also, a rationale: wounds may become fatal, so the outcome must be known.
The hadith is narrated by Ahmad and al-Daraqutni.
3
Examine the proof for the second ruling
The passage cites a report from Ali that he placed the diyah of the insane on his aqilah, saying his intentional and accidental are equal. Also, qiyas: a child is more deserving of leniency than an accidental adult.
The qiyas is based on the principle of mercy.
4
Note the inter-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school allows immediate qisas for wounds, analogizing to qisas for life. The Hanafi school distinguishes because life is certain but wounds may be fatal.
This ikhtilaf is explicitly mentioned in the passage.
5
Understand the legal consequences
For the child/madman: diyah on aqilah (wajib), no kaffarah (because no sin), no deprivation of inheritance (because punishment is not applicable).
The pen is lifted from them, so they are not sinful.

The intentional act of a child and the insane is accidental, and on their aqilah is the diyah, and there is no kaffarah in it, and no deprivation of inheritance. [1] (1) (No qisas for a wound until after healing): This is according to us, and according to al-Shafi'i: immediately, like qisas for life. He executes qisas. And the intentional act of a child and the insane is accidental, and on their aqilah is the diyah, and there is no kaffarah in it, and no deprivation of inheritance. So he does not pay anything. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His saying: except after healing because of what is narrated that he ﷺ forbade that qisas be exacted from a wound until its owner heals. Reported by Ahmad and al-Daraqutni. And because wounds are considered for their outcome, because of the possibility that it may spread to the soul and it becomes apparent that it was killing, so it is not known that it is a wound until healing, so it is established by it. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar quoting from al-Zayla'i. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage states two rulings. First, no qisas (retaliation) for a wound is carried out until the wound heals. This is the Hanafi position; al-Shafi'i holds that qisas can be applied immediately, like for homicide. The proof is a hadith narrated by Ahmad and al-Daraqutni: the Prophet ﷺ forbade qisas for a wound until it heals. Also, wounds may become fatal, and until healing, it is uncertain whether it was a mere wound or a homicide. Second, the deliberate act of a child or an insane person is treated as accidental (khat'a). Therefore, their diyah (blood money) is due on the aqilah (tribal surety), no kaffarah (expiation) is required because they are not sinful (the pen is lifted from them), and they are not deprived of inheritance (since inheritance deprivation is a punishment, and they are not punished). The proof is from Ali (RA) who placed the aql (diyah) of the insane on his aqilah, stating that his deliberate and accidental acts are the same. Also, qiyas: just as an adult accidental offender is given leniency (diyah on aqilah, no kaffarah), a child is even more deserving of leniency. The Hanafi positions are as stated.

Key Terms
جرح (jarḥ): wound, injury that may require qisas or diyah
عاقلة (ʿāqila): tribal surety or group responsible for paying diyah on behalf of the offender
خطأ (khaṭaʾ): accidental, unintentional act

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Accidental (khat'a) with diyah on aqilah
C) Negligent with no liability
D) Intentional but with diyah on the minor's property

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, qisas for wounds is deferred until healing, and the intentional acts of minors and the insane are legally accidental, placing diyah on the aqilah.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

Ruling on Miscarriage: Ghurrah and Diyah for Fetus
States the ruling for causing miscarriage: ghurrah for stillborn, full diyah for live birth then death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for causing a miscarriage, distinguish between the conditions for ghurrah and full diyah, and explain the proof from hadith and the use of istihsan.

Core Idea
The fetus is valued at a ghurrah of 500 dirhams if stillborn; full diyah if born alive then dies — based on the Prophet's judgment, overriding qiyas.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi ruling on miscarriage (janin). It distinguishes two scenarios: stillborn fetus, where a ghurrah is due, and live birth followed by death, where full diyah applies. The ruling is established through istihsan based on the Prophet's hadith, despite qiyas suggesting no liability. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and the basis for preferring the athar over qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios and their rulings
Read the opening sentence: 'if she miscarries it dead, a ghurrah; if alive then dies, a full diyah.' Note the condition that triggers each.
2
Note the amount of ghurrah and its payment terms
The ghurrah is 500 dirhams, paid over three years by the aqilah. The commentary explains that it is the smallest diyah amount and is paid in installments like other limb substitutes.
3
Locate the dalil and understand why qiyas is set aside
The text mentions the hadith from Sahihain about the Prophet's judgment. Qiyas would suggest no payment because the fetus's life is uncertain, but istihsan prefers the athar (prophetic practice and companion reports).
Istihsan is the key principle here.
4
Identify the inter-madhhab difference
Maliki and Shafi'i schools estimate the ghurrah at 600 dirhams. The Hanafi school adheres to 500 based on explicit reports from Umar and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.

فصل في الجنين
ومن ضرب بطن امرأة فألقت جنيناً تَجِبُ غرَةٌ خمسمئة درهم على عاقلته إن ألقت ميتاً ، ودِيَةٌ إن ألقت حياً فمات
[٢]
فصل في الجنين
ومَن ضرب بطن امرأة فألقت جنيناً تَجِبُ غرَةٌ خمسمئة درهم على عاقلتِهِ " إن ألقت ميتاً ، ودِيَةٌ إن ألقت حياً فمات : أي تجبُ الدِّيةُ الكاملة إن ألقت حياً فمات ; لأن موته بسبب الضرب، واعلم أنَّ الغُرَّةَ عندنا تجبُ في سنة ، فإِنَّهُ جَعَلَ الغُرَّةَ على العاقلة في سنة (١) ، وأيضاً هي بدل العضو من وجه ، وما كان بدل العضو يجب في سنة إن كانَ ثلث الدِّية ، أو أقل إلى نصف العشر
[١]قوله : غرة ; - بضم الغين المعجمة وفتح الراء المهملة المشدّدة - : عبارة عن خمسمئة ، هاهنا ، وإنما سمّى الغرّة غرّة ; لأنها أقلّ المقادير في الديات، وأقل الشيء أوله في الوجود، ويسمى غرة بمعنى الأولوية ; لهذا يُسَمَّى أول الشهر غرة، ويسمّى وجه الإنسان غرّة ; لأنه أول شيءٍ يظهر منه ، والقياس أن لا يجب شيء ; لأنه لم يتيقن بحياته. إن قيل : الظاهر أن يكون حيّاً.
قلنا : الظاهر لا يصلح حجّة للاستحقاق ، ووجه الاستحسان ما روي في «الصحيحين» عن أبي هريرة له إنَّ النبي قضى في جنين امرأة من بني لحيان بغرة عبد أو أمة» (٢)
وروى ابن أبي شيبة في «مصنّفه»: عن إسماعيل بن عياش ، عن زيد بن أسلم :

امرأة فقضى رسول الله الله في ولدها بخمسمئة، ونهى عن الحذف». وأخرج أبو داود في سننه» ( : عن إبراهيم النخعي له قال : الغُرّة خمسمئة : يعني درهما، فتركنا القياس بالأثر، وهو حجّة على مالك والشافعي له حيث قدراها بستمئة. هكذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية».
(۱) قال الزيلعي في نصب الراية» (٤) : (۳۸۳) ، وابن حجر في «الدراية»(۲) : ۲۸۲): غريب. (۲) في (صحيح البخاري (٦) : ٢٤٧٨) ، و (صحیح مسلم (۳: ۱۳۰۹)، وغيرهما..
